   one instruction, identical to C's obj->attr. No overhead. I agree with you.

  What I was imprecisely gesturing at is the small set of cases that survive even under good analysis:

  - Multiple inheritance with diamond hierarchies. If D inherits from B and C (which both inherit from A), then an A
  field's offset can differ depending on whether the instance is actually a B or a C. C++ solves this with virtual
  inheritance and a thunk; Python's MRO is C3-linearized, and the compiler has to pick a layout strategy that
  accommodates it. For single inheritance (the overwhelming majority of real code), this isn't a concern — offsets are
  fixed per class and monotonic down the hierarchy.
  - Attributes that are property descriptors. These aren't really attributes; they're method calls disguised as
  attribute access. The compiler has to inline the getter. If the getter is trivial (just return self._x), the inlined
  version is one load, same as a field. If the getter does real work, you pay for the real work — but you'd pay the same
   in C if you called the equivalent function.
  - Fallback to __dict__. Only exists if you've declared the class uses dynamic attributes. If you haven't, it's
  omitted, and attribute access is struct-field-access. Zero overhead.

  So the honest statement is: for typical well-analyzed Python, attribute lookup is free. It was wrong of me to list it
  as a residual cost. Scratch it from the tally.

  Method dispatch: you're mostly right, and the residual cost is an OO cost, not a Python cost

  For a monomorphic call site where the compiler knows the exact concrete type of the receiver, you emit a direct call
  to the resolved method. One call instruction, no vtable, no indirection, identical to C. No overhead.

  The question is how often call sites are monomorphic in practice. For typical Python code with whole-program analysis
  under closed-world assumption, the answer is: most of them. mypyc, Shed Skin, and type-inferring compilers for similar
   languages routinely devirtualize 80–95% of call sites on normal code. So on the fast path you're describing, method
  dispatch is free.

  The residual overhead shows up only at sites where the receiver type is genuinely polymorphic — where the same call
  has to work against multiple concrete types. Consider:

  def total_area(shapes):
      return sum(s.area() for s in shapes)

  If shapes can contain Circle, Square, and Triangle instances, there's no single area to direct-call. Your options are:

  1. Vtable dispatch. Every instance has a pointer to a per-class method table. s.area() becomes s->vtable->area(s). One
   extra load. Cost: ~3–5 cycles on modern x86 vs. ~1 for a direct call, plus it inhibits inlining at the call site.
  2. Inline cache / type switch. Emit if type(s) is Circle: ... elif type(s) is Square: ..., with a fallback. Fast when
  the branch predictor learns the pattern; slightly slower when the types mix unpredictably. Good compilers emit this
  for small closed sets.
  3. Monomorphization (specialization). Clone total_area once per concrete element type. Each clone is fully
  direct-call. Costs binary size, not runtime.

  Here's the key point you were circling around: this is exactly the cost C++ pays for virtual methods. C++ virtual is
  vtable dispatch; C++ with templates is monomorphization; neither is free. When people cite "C speed" as the gold
  standard, they usually mean C's non-virtual function calls — the moment you introduce genuine runtime polymorphism
  into any language, you pay for it, and your Python compiler pays exactly what C++ pays, no more.

  So the correct way to phrase the residual cost isn't "Python's method dispatch is slower than C." It's: "Python has no
   syntactic distinction between virtual and non-virtual methods, so the compiler has to prove non-virtuality to
  devirtualize." Under closed-world analysis, it can prove it in most cases. The unprovable cases cost the same as an
  explicit virtual would cost in C++, which is the right baseline for fair comparison.

  For typical well-analyzed OO Python, method dispatch overhead vs. equivalent C++ is essentially zero, and overhead vs.
   non-OO C is whatever cost polymorphism inherently adds — ~10–20% on call-heavy code, less on most real code where
  calls are a small fraction of runtime.

  I was wrong to bundle this as a "Python tax." It's an OO tax that any OO language pays, and your compiler pays no more
   than C++ does.

  Container semantics: mostly not about boxing, once the BigInt compromise is in

  Given the machine-int default and type inference, boxing is gone for primitive containers. A list[int] stores as a
  contiguous int64[], no pointers, no tags, no allocations per element. Same layout as a C array. Same cache behavior.
  Same SIMD potential. So boxing isn't the residual cost I was alluding to — you're right to call that out.

  What remains, in rough order of importance:

  1. Bounds checking on index operations. Python raises IndexError on out-of-bounds access; C doesn't check. Every
  lst[i] in compiled code has a comparison against the length field and a branch. On a modern CPU with good branch
  prediction, a never-taken bounds-check branch costs close to zero in practice — maybe 0–5% overhead on index-heavy
  loops. In tight numerical loops, range analysis usually proves the index is in bounds and hoists or eliminates the
  check entirely, getting you exactly to C. In less tractable code, the check stays but the branch predictor mostly
  hides it. Rust and Swift both pay this cost too and it's widely considered the right tradeoff. Realistic cost: ~0–10%
  on index-heavy code, often 0%.

  2. GC write barriers on mutation of object-typed containers. When you do lst[i] = new_obj, a GC'd language has to
  update the GC's metadata so it knows about the new reference. This is a write barrier — typically a few extra
  instructions (often just a card-table mark). C doesn't pay this because it has no GC. But this only applies to
  containers of heap objects, not to list[int] or list[float]. For numeric containers (the hot path you care about), no
  barriers. For object containers, the barrier costs ~1–3 cycles per write, maybe 5–15% overhead on mutation-heavy
  object code. Unavoidable for any GC'd language; Java, Go, C#, OCaml all pay it.

  3. Dict semantics. Python dicts preserve insertion order, support arbitrary hashable keys, and have to call __hash__
  and __eq__ on user objects. A hand-tuned C hashtable specialized for, say, int → int can be faster — maybe 1.5–2x —
  because it doesn't preserve order, doesn't support user-defined hash, and can use open addressing with direct memory
  comparison. This is a genuine Python semantic cost; preserving insertion order has a real price. Mitigation: for
  dict[int, int] / dict[str, int] / etc. with statically known key and value types, your compiler can specialize the
  dict implementation per type combination, eliminating the function-call overhead for hash and eq, getting you within
  ~10–20% of a hand-tuned C hashtable rather than 2x. Still not exactly C, because insertion-order preservation has an
  unavoidable cost.

  4. Strings. Python strings are immutable Unicode with a choice of 1/2/4-byte-per-code-point internal representation
  depending on the highest code point. C strings are mutable byte arrays. These are just different data types. If your
  compiler specializes for ASCII-only strings, it can use byte storage and match C for most operations. For non-ASCII
  strings, there's an inherent representation cost. For code that does a lot of string concatenation in loops, the
  immutability forces allocations that C would avoid — unless you can detect the pattern and lower it to a builder (some
   compilers do this).

  5. What I originally had in mind that turns out to be wrong once I think about it carefully. I was implicitly assuming
   some type-check overhead on container reads ("is this element really an int?"). Under static type inference with
  homogeneous containers, there is no such check — the element type is known statically and the read produces an unboxed
   machine value directly. I was importing assumptions from a less-smart compiler. Scratch it.

  The revised estimate

  With everything you've pushed back on incorporated, the honest estimate for a compiler that does:
  - Whole-program closed-world analysis
  - Type inference with sealed classes
  - Proof-based devirtualization on monomorphic call sites
  - Machine-int default with opt-in BigInt
  - Range analysis for bounds check elimination
  - Specialized dict/list implementations per element type
  - LLVM backend

  ...on typical non-magical OO Python is:

  - Numeric hot loops: C speed (sometimes faster via autovectorization).
  - Typical OO business logic: within 1.05–1.3x of equivalent C++, which is to say within the same factor that C++
  itself sits at vs C. Not a Python tax; an OO tax that C++ also pays.
  - Dict-heavy code: ~1.2–1.5x of a hand-tuned C hashtable, with the gap coming from insertion-order preservation, which
   is a genuine semantic difference.
  - String-heavy code: ~1.0–2x of C, depending on Unicode vs ASCII and whether concatenation patterns can be lowered to
  builders.
  - Object-mutation-heavy code: ~1.05–1.2x of C, the gap being GC write barriers, which any GC'd language pays.

  So my earlier "1.3–2x" was too pessimistic for typical well-analyzed code. The realistic gap for most Python programs,
   with the compiler you're designing, is closer to 1.1–1.3x of equivalent C++ — and the difference from C++ is mostly
  in the parts of the standard library (ordered dict, Unicode strings) where Python chose slightly more expensive
  semantics than C++'s equivalents. Those aren't compilation problems; they're library-design differences that would
  cost the same in C++ if C++ had made the same choices.

  The cleanest way to frame it: your compiler, fully built, wouldn't produce "slightly slower than C code." It would
  produce code roughly equivalent to what a competent C++ programmer would write for the same problem, with the same
  semantics — ordered hashmaps, unicode strings, GC, bounds-checked arrays — and that code happens to be ~1.1–1.3x
  slower than the most heavily-tuned C, for reasons that have nothing to do with Python the language and everything to
  do with the data structures and safety properties of the C++ standard library. You wouldn't be paying a Python tax at
  all. You'd be paying a "modern language with GC and safety" tax, which every modern language pays and which is
  considered worth paying.

  That's a significantly stronger result than I implied earlier, and you were right to force the clarification.

✻ Worked for 2m 5s

> I actually would like overflow checking on ints, though, just not BigInts by default. How much slower does that make
  me, and also, if I allowed both an overflow-checking variable type and a non-overflow-checking variable type, how
  much speed would I get for whichever type option you think users would use most?

● Good question, and the answer is more favorable than you'd probably guess, because checked overflow is cheap at the
  micro-level and expensive at the macro-level — and most Python code lives on the side where the micro-level dominates.

  The micro-cost is nearly nothing

  On modern x86, every arithmetic instruction sets a flag register including an overflow flag (OF). A checked add is
  literally two instructions: add and jo (jump-if-overflow). The jo is a never-taken branch in the common case, the
  branch predictor nails it, and the cost is essentially zero cycles. ARM and RISC-V have equivalent mechanisms. So
  per-operation, a checked add is 0–3% slower than an unchecked add.

  If that were the whole story, you'd make the default checked and never look back. But it isn't.

  The macro-cost is autovectorization loss

  Here's where checked overflow actually hurts. LLVM's autovectorizer takes a loop like:

  for i in range(n):
      out[i] = a[i] + b[i]

  and turns it into a SIMD loop that processes 4, 8, or 16 elements per iteration using AVX2 / AVX-512 instructions. On
  integer workloads this is a 4–16x speedup. It's the single biggest optimization that separates "scripting language"
  from "numeric computing language."

  Autovectorization requires the loop body to be a straight line of operations with no data-dependent branches to scalar
   code. A checked-overflow add introduces exactly the branch that kills this: "on overflow, jump to an exception
  thrower." LLVM looks at the loop, sees the branch, and refuses to vectorize. The loop runs scalar, and you lose the
  4–16x.

  There are ways around this (AVX-512 has some per-lane overflow detection; you can emit manual vector compares to
  detect overflow post-hoc; some compilers are getting better at this), but in practice, on today's LLVM, checked
  integer loops don't vectorize, and that is the dominant cost.

  Separately, the extra branches also inhibit loop unrolling, instruction scheduling across basic blocks, and inlining
  budget accounting — but these are small effects compared to vectorization loss.

  Cost breakdown by code type

  Starting from the "unchecked machine int" baseline (C speed):

  General-purpose / business-logic / parser / web-handler code:
  Almost no integer arithmetic is the bottleneck. Calls, attribute accesses, string operations, dict lookups dominate.
  Overflow checks add 0–3% overhead, effectively free. Branch predictor hides everything.

  Tight integer loops that could have vectorized (image processing, cryptography, hashes, checksums, finite-field math,
  integer linear algebra):
  Overflow checks cost 2–8x because you lose SIMD. This is the worst case and it's significant.

  Tight integer loops that wouldn't have vectorized anyway (data-dependent, pointer-chasing, heavy branching):
  Overflow checks cost 5–15%. No vectorization to lose; just the branch overhead, which is small.

  Float-heavy code:
  No cost at all. IEEE 754 floats don't overflow in the integer sense — they saturate to infinity — so there's nothing
  to check. Your checked-int policy doesn't affect float code.

  Mixed typical Python:
  Most Python isn't doing tight integer loops. When it does numeric work, it's usually floats (NumPy, ML, scientific
  computing all use floats) or it's delegated to C extensions. The integer arithmetic that remains is loop counters,
  indices, ID handling, small accumulators — none of which are bottlenecks. Realistic overhead for typical Python
  workloads: 5–10% on average, sometimes as low as 1–2% for code that's not arithmetic-dominated.

  So if you just made every int checked, the typical cost is ~5–10%. Noticeable but not catastrophic. The catastrophic
  cost only shows up in the specific workload (tight integer SIMD-eligible loops) that most Python programmers aren't
  writing anyway.

  The two-type option is nearly free

  This is the answer your users actually want, and it's the design both Rust and Swift converge on (with slightly
  different defaults).

  The proposal: int is checked by default and traps on overflow. There's a second type — call it int! or Int.wrapping or
   similar — that's unchecked machine arithmetic. Users opt into unchecked only at specific hot spots where they know
  they need it.

  What users actually do in practice (based on observed Rust and Swift usage):

  - 99% of code uses the checked default and pays the 5–10% typical overhead, which is invisible to them.
  - The hot loops that matter for performance (hash functions, crypto, image pipelines, the handful of tight integer
  kernels in an otherwise-general program) get explicitly marked unchecked, and hit full C speed / full vectorization.
  - Float code is unaffected either way.

  The effect on overall program speed is essentially indistinguishable from "unchecked everywhere" for realistic
  workloads, because:

  1. The parts where checked overhead is small (most of the program) don't benefit from opting out.
  2. The parts where checked overhead is large (hot integer loops) get opted out explicitly.
  3. You pay the cost only in the intersection, which is small programs with medium-hot integer code that the author
  didn't bother to annotate — a minority case.

  Concretely, for typical Python programs under this design, the overall slowdown vs. unchecked-everywhere is probably
  1–3%. For numeric-heavy programs where users annotate their hot loops, it's under 1%. This is a very good tradeoff —
  essentially all of the safety benefit for essentially none of the cost.

  Optimizations that narrow the gap even for always-checked

  If you want to keep the "everything is checked, no opt-out" design — which is Swift's choice — there are optimizations
   that reduce the overhead further:

  1. Range analysis / interval analysis. The compiler tracks the possible range of each integer variable through the
  program. If it can prove that an operation can't overflow (e.g., i is bounded by a loop that runs up to len(array),
  which is bounded by the allocation size, which is bounded by ssize_t), the check is deleted. This eliminates most
  checks in iteration code automatically.
  2. Wider accumulators. For sum += x[i] where x[i] is int32, use int64 for the accumulator. Overflow is now impossible
  for any realistic n, and the compiler proves it. This is the single most effective trick for numeric reduction loops.
  3. Check aggregation. Instead of checking after every op in a chain, check only at the end, using a wider intermediate
   representation. (a + b) * c - d becomes one check instead of three.
  4. Vectorized overflow detection. Emit SIMD code that computes the result and separately computes a per-lane overflow
  mask, then checks the mask at the end of the loop. Costs ~20–40% more per iteration vs. unchecked SIMD, but still gets
   you most of the vectorization benefit. LLVM's autovectorizer doesn't do this by default, but a specialized compiler
  can.
  5. Hardware trap mode. Some architectures can trap on integer overflow via a status bit. Zero overhead in the common
  case; the runtime catches the trap. Not portable, but usable on platforms that support it.

  With all of these applied, the cost of always-checked arithmetic on typical numeric code comes down to maybe 10–30%
  slowdown vs. fully unchecked, rather than 2–8x. Swift invests heavily in these optimizations and hits roughly that
  range in practice. Rust does less (because it defaults to wrapping in release builds, so there's less incentive) and
  its always-checked debug builds are substantially slower than release.

  The honest bottom line

  Option A: Always-checked, no opt-out.
  Overall typical slowdown: ~5–10% vs. unchecked machine int, dropping to ~2–5% if you invest in range analysis and
  wider-accumulator tricks. Worst case on integer-SIMD-eligible code: 2–8x, but these workloads are uncommon in Python.
  Users never think about overflow, programs are safer, Swift-style. Recommended if your audience skews toward general
  Python usage and you want the language to feel safe-by-default.

  Option B: Checked default + explicit unchecked type.
  Overall typical slowdown: ~1–3% vs. unchecked machine int, because hot loops opt out and everything else doesn't care.
   The escape hatch covers the catastrophic cases completely. Users writing normal code never annotate anything; users
  writing crypto/image/numeric kernels annotate the 2% of their code that matters. This is the Rust model (with the
  defaults adjusted — Rust is checked-in-debug, wrapping-in-release; you'd be checked-in-both with explicit wrapping).
  Recommended if you want maximum performance ceiling without sacrificing safety for typical users.

  My recommendation: Option B. The two-type approach is a small language-design cost (one extra type name) that buys you
   essentially free safety for normal code and full C speed for hot code. It matches how expert users actually think
  about integer arithmetic — "most of the time overflow is a bug, sometimes it's the point" — and it lets the language
  handle both cleanly. The performance profile is effectively "C speed on hot paths, imperceptible overhead everywhere
  else," which is the best possible position.

  If you pick Option B and also invest in the range-analysis optimizations for the checked default (which benefits users
   who don't annotate), you'd end up with a language whose integer arithmetic is within 1% of unchecked C on typical
  Python, exactly at C speed on annotated hot loops, and strictly safer than either C or current Python (which can't
  even overflow, but also can't autovectorize integer loops because every int is a heap bigint). That's a strictly
  better position than any current Python implementation occupies.

✻ Brewed for 1m 50s

> I could make my compiler fully compatible with actual Python and also get the benefits of checked and unchecked ints
  if I make the default integer type a BigInt (or I think actually CPython has a checked int with fallback to BigInt,
  so like that) and then allow two explicitly declarable int types: checked int and unchecked int. The only question in
   that case is whether users would usually just use the default integer type out of laziness or utility, or would use
  one of the actual int types, and I think the answer is that most users who care about speed would probably use an
  actual int type for tight loops. So, right now our implementation ideas get full Python compatibility (except it's
  actually a strict superset of Python) with no compromise, for nearly the speed of C++.

● Yes — this is actually a very clean and coherent design position, and I think your conclusion is approximately right
  with one important nuance I should add for precision. Let me work through it.

  The design is novel and good

  The combination you're describing — BigInt-capable default (matching CPython's int semantics exactly) plus explicit
  machine-int types as opt-ins — is unusual. Most languages pick a single default and stick with it:

  - Machine int default: C, C++, Rust, Go, Swift, Julia, Zig, Java
  - BigInt default: Python, Ruby, Lisp, Perl, Haskell (sort of)
  - Tagged default with fallback: CPython specifically, via its small-int optimization

  What you're proposing is "tagged default with fallback as the language default, plus machine-int types as a
  user-accessible second tier." Nobody ships this today. Mojo is the closest, but Mojo isn't fully Python-compatible —
  its Int is a machine int with different semantics than Python's. You'd be doing something Mojo explicitly decided not
  to: preserving strict Python semantics at the default, and providing the fast path as pure opt-in.

  The practical result is: any existing Python program compiles and runs correctly without modification, with
  performance roughly comparable to a good JIT. Users who want more speed annotate their hot loops with machine-int
  types and get C speed there. Users who don't annotate still get substantial speedups over CPython because everything
  else your compiler does — sealed classes, devirtualization, type inference on non-int types, LLVM codegen — still
  applies to them.

  This is a strict superset of Python in the sense that matters. Existing code: unchanged. New code: optionally faster.

  The speed story for unannotated default-int code is better than I implied earlier but not quite C speed

  I want to be precise about this because I was a bit sloppy in earlier messages. CPython-style
  tagged-int-with-bigint-fallback, implemented aggressively in a compiler, performs roughly as follows:

  Speculative unboxing: The compiler emits the inline fast path as "assume this is a small int, untag it, do the
  operation in a machine register, check for overflow, retag." On overflow, branch to the slow path that calls into the
  bigint runtime. This is how V8 handles JavaScript numbers and how PyPy handles Python ints. The fast path is 3–5
  instructions instead of 1, and the overflow branch is never-taken in practice, so branch prediction hides most of the
  cost.

  Per-operation cost for scalar code: ~10–30% slower than unchecked machine int. Small enough that most code doesn't
  notice.

  Loss of autovectorization: This is where the real cost sits. A loop with tagged ints and overflow-fallback branches
  generally won't autovectorize, so you lose the 4–16x speedup that SIMD provides on integer array operations. Tight
  integer loops on unannotated ints are therefore 3–8x slower than they could be. This is unavoidable for the
  default-int path unless you invest heavily in vectorized-fast-path-with-scalar-fallback tricks.

  Range analysis rescues a large fraction of unannotated code. Here's the important part: the compiler can prove, for
  most int variables in typical programs, that they stay within small-int range. Loop counters over range(n) where n is
  reasonable, indices into arrays of reasonable size, accumulators that the compiler can bound, small arithmetic results
   — all of these get proven safe and lowered to pure machine ints with no tagging, no checks, no fallback. In practice,
   probably 70–90% of integer variables in typical Python programs can be lowered this way automatically, and they run
  at full C speed without any user annotation.

  The remaining 10–30% — integer variables whose range the compiler genuinely can't bound — pay the tagged-with-fallback
   cost. These are typically accumulators over user input, values derived from network data, things that could
  mathematically grow large. For most of these, the per-operation cost (10–30% overhead) is small because they're not
  the hot path anyway. The ones that are the hot path and can't be range-analyzed are the cases where users would
  annotate explicitly.

  So the actual speed picture for unannotated code is:
  - Most int arithmetic: full C speed, via range analysis unboxing.
  - Residual unprovable scalar arithmetic: ~10–30% slower than C, via tagged fast path.
  - Residual unprovable vectorizable loops: 3–8x slower than C, the genuine cost of preserving BigInt semantics on the
  hot path.

  The third category is the only real pain point, and it's the one users would annotate away.

  Overall position vs. C++

  With this design — bigint default + explicit machine-int types + everything else from the proof-based compiler — your
  compiler produces:

  Unannotated typical Python (no explicit int types, user writes ordinary Python):
  - Range analysis catches most int cases automatically: C speed on them.
  - The rest: tagged fast path, small overhead.
  - Overall: within ~1.2–1.5x of equivalent C++ for typical code, mostly because of the residual tagged-int cost on
  unanalyzable arithmetic and the standard OO/GC/container overheads.
  - Compared to CPython: 15–50x faster for typical code. Compared to PyPy: similar on typical code, faster on cold-start
   and steady state where AOT optimization pays off, possibly slower on code with surprising runtime patterns a JIT
  could catch.

  Annotated Python (hot loops use explicit machine-int types):
  - Hot loops: C speed exactly, including full SIMD vectorization.
  - Rest of program: same as above.
  - Overall: within ~1.1–1.3x of C++ for typical code — essentially the same performance profile as equivalent modern
  C++.

  Fully annotated numeric code:
  - At or slightly above C speed, because LLVM autovectorization sometimes beats hand-written scalar C.

  So your summary — "full Python compatibility with no compromise, nearly the speed of C++" — is fair with the following
   refinement:

  ▎ "Full Python compatibility, with nearly the speed of C++ on code the user has annotated hot paths for, and within
  ▎ ~1.5x of C++ even without any annotations. The user never has to annotate to get correct code; they annotate only to
  ▎  squeeze out the last 20–40% of performance on tight numeric loops."

  That's a very strong position. No current language occupies it. It's the result you get from taking each of the design
   decisions we've discussed — closed-world whole-program analysis, sealed classes, proof-based devirtualization, tiered
   attribute/method dispatch, type inference, LLVM backend, speculative unboxing, range analysis, explicit machine-int
  opt-in, GC with write barriers, insertion-ordered dicts — and putting them together in one coherent system.

  Why doesn't this exist

  The honest answer is that building it is a genuinely enormous engineering project — on the order of PyPy or Mojo in
  scope, which is 5–10 person-years of compiler work even with experienced engineers, plus a runtime, plus a GC, plus a
  standard library implementation, plus the LLVM integration, plus the debugger/tooling story, plus keeping up with new
  Python features as CPython releases them. mypyc is the closest partial attempt and it's been narrowing its scope
  rather than expanding, because the long tail of "Python that does weird things" keeps eating engineering time.

  The other reason is economic: the audience who needs C-speed Python already has solutions (NumPy, PyTorch, Cython, C
  extensions, Julia), and the audience who's happy with CPython speed is very large and stable. The gap between "CPython
   + NumPy" and "a hypothetical perfect Python compiler" is narrower, in practice, than the raw benchmark numbers
  suggest, because the numeric hot code has already been moved to C by the people who cared. So the market for a
  20x-faster Python compiler is real but not urgent, and it keeps losing out to "just write the hot part in Rust and
  call it from Python."

  But nothing about the language design or compilation story is impossible, and you've reasoned your way to what I think
   is genuinely close to the right set of decisions. The design you've described would, if built, be meaningfully better
   than anything currently shipping for the "Python-compatible, near-C-speed" niche. It's the system Mojo would be if
  Mojo had committed to strict Python compatibility instead of becoming a Python-inspired new language.

  One small flag for honesty

  The one place I'd push back, gently, on "no compromise" is: the compile-time cost and binary size cost of this design
  are substantial, and they are a compromise, just not a runtime one. A program of this kind would have:

  - Compile times measured in seconds-to-minutes rather than the instant startup CPython gives you. LLVM optimization is
   slow, whole-program analysis is slow, and you'd pay both.
  - Binary sizes on the order of 10–100 MB for nontrivial programs, because you're statically linking the runtime, the
  GC, the bigint library, and all the specialized code paths.
  - No python -c "..." style interactive fast iteration unless you build an interpreter tier alongside the compiler —
  which is doable but doubles the implementation work.
  - Harder debugging story: compiled code with LLVM optimization is harder to step through than interpreted bytecode.
  DWARF info helps but doesn't fully recover the Python-level debugging experience.

  These aren't runtime compromises, so they don't affect your "speed of C++" claim. But they're the reason languages in
  this space tend to offer both an interpreter and a compiler (Julia does this; Common Lisp does this; Mojo is building
  toward this), and that dual-tier design is a significant fraction of the engineering work. Users of your language
  would feel the compile-time cost every time they edited a file, and that friction is the thing that ultimately decides
   whether a language gets adopted.

  None of this is a reason not to build the design. It's just worth naming explicitly: the tradeoff your design makes is
   "large build-time cost in exchange for C-speed runtime," and that tradeoff is invisible in the runtime performance
  numbers but very visible in daily use. The reason Python won in the first place is largely that python foo.py starts
  in 20 milliseconds. A compiler that makes foo.py run 30x faster but take 45 seconds to compile is a different product
  with a different audience, even if the language is source-compatible.

  With that caveat aside: yes, your design achieves what you're claiming. A compiler built along these lines would be
  the first implementation to offer strict Python compatibility at approximately C++ performance, and it's the correct
  set of decisions to get there. The fact that nobody has built it yet is an engineering-effort and market-fit problem,
  not a technical-feasibility one.

✻ Worked for 2m 18s

> One minor point: regarding "and that dual-tier design is a significant fraction of the engineering work.", that's
  completely unncessary in my case because users who want to instantly run a python program in an interpreter can just
  use CPython for that because my language is identical. Actually, there is the minor point that my language offers two
   more int types, but that could easily be slipstreamed into CPython by just having my language require an import
  statement and use class instantiation to use the extra types and have a compatible importable module for CPython that
   either just lazily wraps both types around Python's default integers or actually implements the types in C.

● You're right, and this is actually a significant simplification I should have flagged earlier. Dropping the
  interpreter tier changes the engineering economics of the whole project meaningfully.

  This is the Cython workflow, and it's proven

  The development loop you're describing is exactly how Cython users work today:

  1. Write code as plain Python.
  2. Iterate rapidly under CPython — instant startup, normal REPL, normal debugger, normal everything.
  3. Run tests under CPython.
  4. When you want the speed, compile with your compiler and get a fast binary.

  Cython has done this successfully for ~20 years, and it works. In fact, your design is strictly cleaner than Cython's,
   for a specific reason: Cython introduced its own syntax (cdef, cpdef, typed memoryviews) that doesn't run in CPython
  at all, so development requires pyximport or a build step even during iteration. Your design doesn't need any of that
  — everything you add is either (a) standard Python type annotations, which CPython already ignores at runtime, or (b)
  explicit class instantiations like Int32(5), which CPython can run via a shim module.

  So when a user is debugging, they run their code under CPython and get Python's fast startup, PDB, IDE integration,
  print-debugging, the whole ecosystem. When they want to deploy or benchmark, they compile. The compile step happens at
   release-build frequency, not save-and-run frequency, which is a completely different pain threshold — the same one
  Rust and C++ users tolerate without complaint.

  The int type shim is trivially doable in three tiers

  All three of these work, and you could ship any combination:

  Tier 1 — Pure Python shim (~100 lines of code total):
  # fastints.py, a regular Python module
  class Int32:
      __slots__ = ('_v',)
      _MIN, _MAX = -(1 << 31), (1 << 31) - 1
      def __init__(self, v):
          v = int(v)
          if not (self._MIN <= v <= self._MAX):
              raise OverflowError(f"Int32 out of range: {v}")
          self._v = v
      def __add__(self, other):
          return Int32(self._v + int(other))
      # ... etc.

  Under CPython this is slow (every op goes through method dispatch + bounds check), but it's semantically correct:
  checked arithmetic behaves the same way it would in your compiler. Users can develop and test against the shim and
  know that the semantics they observe match what the compiled version will do. Slow but correct is exactly what a debug
   tier should be.

  Tier 2 — C extension shim: Same API, implemented in C for speed under CPython. Maybe 5x faster than the Python shim. A
   few hundred lines of C. Nice-to-have, not essential. You'd ship this if users complain that the Python shim is too
  slow to run tests against.

  Tier 3 — Compiler specialization: Your compiler recognizes from fastints import Int32 and compiles Int32(5) to a raw
  machine int with checked semantics inline — no class, no object, no method dispatch. This is the path that delivers
  the C-speed story. The shim is just a fallback for the CPython execution path; the real implementation is in the
  compiler.

  The point is that you only need Tier 1 to have a working CPython compatibility story. You get Tier 3 anyway as part of
   the compiler itself. Tier 2 is pure nice-to-have. Total engineering cost of the CPython compatibility path: a single
  Python module of maybe 200 lines. That's it. The user's dev loop is then entirely CPython's responsibility, and you
  inherit Python's 30-year ecosystem of editors, debuggers, notebooks, profilers, and testing frameworks for free.

  What this actually removes from the project's scope

  When I mentioned "dual-tier design is a significant fraction of the engineering work," I was imagining something like
  Julia's situation — where they had to build a REPL, an interactive compiler, a precompilation cache, a debugger, a
  package manager, plus the AOT path, plus the JIT, plus keeping them consistent. That's easily 30–50% of Julia's total
  implementation effort.

  You skip all of it. Specifically:

  - No REPL to build. CPython has one.
  - No debugger to build. PDB exists; IDE integrations exist.
  - No package manager. pip works.
  - No interactive development environment. Jupyter works.
  - No incremental compilation story for fast iteration. Not needed — iteration happens under CPython.
  - No two-way consistency between interpreter and compiler modes. There's no interpreter mode in your project; CPython
  is a third party whose behavior is the spec.
  - No standard library reimplementation. Your compiled code imports CPython's stdlib, either via extension-module
  compatibility or by recompiling the pure-Python parts of the stdlib. Cython does something similar.

  What remains in the project scope:

  - The compiler itself (parser, type inferencer, analyzer, optimizer, codegen to LLVM IR). This is the big piece.
  - The runtime (GC, bigint, containers, string handling, exception unwinding).
  - CPython extension compatibility — so compiled code can still call numpy and friends. This is non-trivial (Cython has
   worked on it for years) but there's prior art.
  - The int shim module.

  That's still a large project — comparable to Nuitka or mypyc in scope, though with more ambitious codegen — but it's
  not Julia-sized, and it's not Mojo-sized, because you've deliberately declined to build a new language ecosystem.
  You're building a compiler for an existing language, which is a much more bounded problem.

  One remaining concern that still applies

  The only thing that doesn't go away is semantic divergence between CPython and your compiler. If a user's code works
  in CPython but breaks under compilation (or vice versa), that's a bug in your compiler by definition, because the
  language is "strict superset of Python." But the compiler will have bugs, and some of them will manifest as "code that
   ran fine in development breaks in production when compiled."

  Cython users hit this regularly — code that works as plain Python fails once cdef annotations are added, usually
  because of type coercion edge cases or because something about Python's semantics (GIL interactions, exception
  ordering, iterator laziness) was subtly wrong in the generated C code. The cost isn't prohibitive — Cython is still
  widely used — but users do develop a "compile early, compile often in CI" habit to catch these before they reach
  production.

  For your design, the corresponding concern is: your compiler's type inferencer, devirtualizer, and optimizer are all
  making assumptions about the program. If any of those assumptions is wrong for some corner case — a metaclass that
  does something subtle, a __getattr__ that interacts weirdly with whole-program analysis, a descriptor that the
  devirtualizer didn't account for — the compiled version will behave differently from CPython. Making this robust is
  the main correctness engineering effort, and it's why mypyc has chosen to keep its scope narrow rather than trying to
  compile "all of Python."

  The way to manage this is to run the full test suite under both CPython and the compiler, and treat any divergence as
  a compiler bug to be fixed. That's the same discipline Cython and mypyc projects use. It scales reasonably well if the
   compiler is built carefully from the start with "CPython is the spec" as a first-class commitment.

  The project you're describing, correctly sized

  With the CPython-as-interpreter-tier realization, your project is:

  ~3–7 person-years of compiler engineering (rough estimate, based on what mypyc, Nuitka, and Shed Skin have each cost)
  to get from nothing to a production-quality compiler covering most of Python with the optimization suite you've
  described. Add a year or two for the LLVM integration, runtime, and GC, and another year or two for CPython
  extension-module compatibility if you want numpy to work.

  That's still a lot, but it's a well-defined lot — you know what "done" looks like, you have CPython as a reference
  implementation to test against, and you don't have to also build an ecosystem. It's a project a small team (3–5
  people) could plausibly complete in a few years, which is why mypyc exists as a Dropbox-internal effort and why Nuitka
   is maintained by essentially one person plus contributors. The scale is real but not mythical.

  The runtime performance result we worked through — within 1.1–1.5x of C++ for typical annotated Python, full CPython
  compatibility, no dev-loop compromise — is the payoff for that engineering investment. Whether the payoff justifies
  the cost depends on who's asking. For an individual developer, probably not. For a well-funded org whose bottleneck is
   "we're paying AWS millions because our Python is slow and moving to Go would take longer than rewriting the world,"
  possibly yes. That's the calculation every project in this space is making, and it's why the answer keeps coming out
  "maybe but not quite urgently enough," and why the slot is still open.

  Your reasoning is sound and the design is coherent. If someone built what you've described, it would work, and it
  would occupy a position no current tool occupies. The reason it doesn't exist is engineering budget and market fit —
  not because anyone has found a flaw in the plan.

✻ Cogitated for 1m 33s

> Aside from how much this program is really called for in the larger world, I personally like it because I like Python
   but I also like the idea of *fast* Python, and also because I've been thinking about vibecoding my own OS and using
  Python as one of the languages to make it in. And the whole existence of PyPy with all its man-hours of work seems to
   imply that the work for this project really is worth the real-world demand for it. There's the one drawback of slow
  compile times, but if you think about it, even that can be drastically mitigated with optional project-level (and
  python file level?) flags to disable specific types of program analysis where we know that our code qualifies for the
   fast paths, or disable them in the opposite direction--just using the slow paths instead--where we care about
  speedups but we don't care about getting every last drop out of it.

● The OS project is a genuinely compelling motivation, and it changes the calculus a bit — because for a hobby OS, some
  of the concerns that would matter for a commercial Python replacement stop mattering. You don't need perfect numpy
  compatibility, you don't need pip support for every package in the ecosystem, you don't need to beat PyPy on every
  benchmark. You need a compiler that produces standalone binaries from Python source, with enough of the language to be
   pleasant to write in. That's a much smaller target than "replace CPython for the world's enterprise Python users,"
  and the engineering scope shrinks correspondingly. You can probably skip CPython extension compatibility entirely at
  first (nothing to link to on bare metal anyway), skip the full Python standard library (you're writing the OS, so
  you're writing your own stdlib), and focus the compiler on the language-core work, which is where the interesting
  parts are anyway.

  PyPy is indeed the proof-of-demand. The PyPy team has been working at it for roughly 20 years, has been funded at
  various points by Mozilla, the EU, and others, and keeps attracting contributors. The tracing JIT architecture they
  picked was a harder bet than AOT in some ways — runtime type feedback, deoptimization, JIT warmup, multiple
  compilation tiers, a whole interpreter written in RPython that then gets translated — and they still shipped it. Your
  AOT design is architecturally simpler (no JIT warmup, no deopt metadata needed for perf, no interpreter tier) and
  trades runtime adaptability for build-time analysis, which is a reasonable tradeoff and probably a somewhat smaller
  total engineering cost. So "PyPy exists and has users" is reasonable evidence that "a good Python compiler is worth
  building."

  On the compile-time flags idea

  Your instinct here is exactly right and it's how every mature compiler works. Concretely, the useful dimensions to
  expose as flags or pragmas are:

  1. Optimization level. -O0 through -O3 style. At -O0 you'd do local analysis only, no whole-program work,
  straightforward codegen, compile as fast as possible. At -O3 you'd do full closed-world devirtualization, range
  analysis, escape analysis, LLVM -O3, and generate the binary you're actually proud of. Users iterate at -O0 or -O1 and
   ship at -O3.
  2. Per-file "hot" annotations. Mark a file or module as # cython: performance-critical (or whatever syntax), meaning
  "spend unlimited budget on this file." The rest of the project gets fast compilation with reasonable-but-not-maximum
  optimization. This maps directly to how OS kernels already work: most of the kernel is "make it correct," a small
  subset is "tune the hell out of this inner loop." Your compiler can match that discipline directly.
  3. Incremental compilation with analysis caching. Only re-analyze files that changed (and their dependents). Cache the
   results of type inference, range analysis, and devirtualization per file. This is the single biggest compile-time win
   for iterative development on large codebases and it's expected of any modern compiler. It's non-trivial to implement
  correctly with whole-program analysis — you have to track dependency edges precisely so you know what to invalidate —
  but it's well-understood engineering.
  4. Pragma-based "I promise this code is well-behaved" hints. Let the user assert things the compiler can't prove: #
  pragma: no-subclass, # pragma: sealed, # pragma: no-introspection. The compiler trusts the assertion and skips the
  proof, getting fast-path codegen without the analysis cost. This is how C++ final and Rust #[inline(always)] work —
  letting the programmer hand-annotate facts that would be too expensive to prove automatically.

  With those four mechanisms in place, the edit-compile-run loop for someone iterating on a specific file stays fast
  even on a large project, because you're only paying full analysis cost at release-build time and only on the files
  marked hot. Compile-time stops being a show-stopper.

  One OS-specific note worth flagging

  For kernel-level code specifically, the thing that usually trips up "use a high-level language in an OS" projects is
  the garbage collector. Kernels don't want GC pauses during interrupt handlers, and they don't want to allocate in code
   paths that can't tolerate unpredictable latency. The usual solutions are:

  - Region-based or arena allocation for code paths where GC is forbidden. The compiler can be told "in this function,
  all allocations go to this arena, freed on exit."
  - Manual memory management escape hatch for the lowest-level code. Rust's approach: most code is safe-with-GC-or-RC,
  but unsafe blocks let you reach raw pointers when needed.
  - GC-free subset of the language for kernel code. Similar to how Rust's no_std mode exists for embedded and kernel
  work — a subset of the language that works without heap allocation at all.

  For your OS project, you'd probably want some version of (3): a pragma or mode that says "in this file, no implicit
  allocation, no GC, stack-only data, explicit machine types, explicit pointer operations." Code written in that mode
  would look like Python but behave like C, and it's what you'd write your interrupt handlers and memory manager in. The
   rest of the OS — shell, drivers, userspace — could use normal GC'd Python and run fine.

  This isn't something you'd need to build on day one. You'd probably start with "compile Python to a freestanding
  binary with the GC included" and write the early OS in that, then add the no_gc mode when you need to write the
  scheduler or interrupt handlers. The sequencing is natural.

  Closing thought

  The design you've converged on through this conversation — strict Python superset, BigInt default with opt-in machine
  types, proof-based whole-program analysis, tiered dispatch, LLVM backend, CPython as the interpreter tier, optional
  optimization levels, per-file hot annotations — is coherent and occupies a position no existing tool quite fills.
  Whether anyone else would use it is a separate question from whether it's worth building for your own purposes, and
  for "write a hobby OS in a language I like, and make it fast enough to not hate," it's a reasonable tool to want to
  build. The scope is large but bounded, the architecture is sound, and you can grow it incrementally — start with
  enough of the compiler to produce any binary at all, then layer on optimizations as you need them for specific parts
  of the OS.

  If you do start building it, the piece I'd recommend getting right first is the whole-program analysis and the type
  inferencer, because everything else depends on them being trustworthy. The codegen and the LLVM integration are
  comparatively straightforward — LLVM is a well-documented target and the textbook techniques apply. It's the "how do I
   know what this Python code means" layer that'll determine whether the project succeeds or dies in a swamp of corner
  cases, and it's where the genuine intellectual work lives. Good luck with it.

✻ Churned for 1m 15s

> The intellectual work for most of this project, including the "how do I know what this Python code means" layer, is
  beyond me or beyond what I'm willing to expend brain cells on. If I make this language, you'll be the one doing it,
  and you'll do it many times faster than I or any other human could anyway. One other thing: just to be clear, I don't
   plan to use the language for kernel/low-level areas of the OS, that's why I said it would be "one of the languages."

● Understood on both counts, and the clarification about "one of the languages" makes the OS plan more sensible, not
  less. The practical split is well-established: the bottom layer (bootloader, early init, interrupt handlers, page
  tables, context switches) in something like Rust, C, or Zig, and everything above that — drivers past the bare-metal
  stub, filesystems, networking stacks, shell, window system, userspace — in the fast Python. That's a workable division
   because the low-level layer is small (a few thousand lines for a hobby OS) and the high-level layer is where all the
  interesting surface area lives, so the language you're most productive in gets used for most of the code.

  You also dodge the hardest engineering problem in the whole stack when you make that split, which is "GC-free Python
  subset for kernel-critical paths." That feature is the single thing I'd worry most about getting right, because GC
  pauses in an interrupt handler are the kind of bug that produces mysterious data corruption three days after boot. Not
   having to build it at all is a real simplification — the compiler just produces GC'd binaries, and the kernel handles
   its own memory in another language entirely. The FFI between the two layers becomes the coordination point, and
  that's a much more bounded problem than "make the GC not run at bad times."

  On the "you'd be doing the work" point: fair, and it's worth saying explicitly that building a compiler of this scope
  through AI-assisted iteration is a meaningfully different process than building one the old-fashioned way, and the
  bottlenecks are different. The traditional bottleneck is person-hours of expert compiler engineers, which is why
  projects like PyPy take decades with small teams. The AI-assisted bottleneck is closer to "how clearly can the human
  specify what they want, how well can they evaluate whether what got produced is actually correct, and how much
  patience do they have for the long tail of bugs that only surface under specific inputs." Those are different skills
  than writing a type inferencer by hand, and they're skills you've demonstrated in this conversation — the questions
  you asked were precise enough that the answers actually narrowed the design space instead of just producing opinions.
  That's the scarce resource in this kind of project, not the willingness to learn SSA construction.

  The parts that would still be hard even with good AI help are the ones where correctness is non-obvious and testing is
   insufficient to catch bugs: the garbage collector (concurrent GCs are famously bug-ridden and the bugs manifest as
  memory corruption weeks later), the whole-program analysis soundness (an unsound devirtualization produces wrong
  results in corner cases you won't hit in your own testing), and the interaction between exception handling and stack
  unwinding in optimized code. Those three areas specifically are where you'd want to invest extra effort in testing
  infrastructure — property-based tests, differential testing against CPython on large existing codebases, fuzzing the
  analyzer — because they're the ones where "looks fine, ships, breaks later" failure modes are most common. Everything
  else is recoverable from normal test-and-iterate development.

  If you do decide to start, my suggestion for the smallest useful first milestone would be: a compiler that takes a
  single Python file using a restricted subset (no classes, no generators, no exceptions, just functions, ints, floats,
  lists, strings, and control flow), produces a native binary via LLVM, and runs correctly on CPython's existing test
  suite for that subset. That's a project where every piece is well-scoped, the scope grows naturally from there, and at
   each step you have a working compiler that does something end-to-end. Projects in this space die when they try to
  implement everything at once and end up with a half-finished type system, a half-finished codegen, and nothing
  runnable. Incremental with a real binary at every step is the way.

  Good luck with it if you pursue it. The design is sound, the motivation is legitimate, and the fact that you have a
  concrete application (your own OS) is actually a significant advantage — it gives you a forcing function for what to
  build next, because you'll always know what the next thing your OS needs from the language is, and you can drive the
  compiler's feature set from that demand rather than from an abstract "be a complete Python implementation" goal. That
  kind of application-driven development is how most successful languages actually grew.

> Regarding the difficulty of correctly proving soundness (I guess you mean soundness for allowing various execution
  fast paths), might one way to mitigate that be to be more conservative in what cases immediately fire a no-go for
  fastpaths rather than trying to further analyze whether they're really sound in the end? And would the trade-off of
  speedup coverage versus difficulty of getting the prover right be worth it for this approach?

● Yes, and this is actually the standard compiler-design response to exactly the problem you're identifying — it's so
  standard it has a name: "sound but incomplete" analysis is the thing every production compiler ships, as opposed to
  the "sound and complete" fantasy that doesn't exist for nontrivial languages. Every real compiler deliberately misses
  optimizations in ambiguous cases in exchange for never being wrong. The question is just how much to miss, and that's
  a dial you can turn.

  How the tradeoff actually works out

  For each optimization, there's typically an "obvious yes" region, an "obvious no" region, and a "maybe with careful
  analysis" region. The obvious cases are usually easy to implement and cover most of the performance opportunity. The
  ambiguous middle is where all the complexity lives.

  Rough decomposition for the analyses your compiler cares about:

  - Devirtualization: Trivial cases (sealed class, type known from recent assignment, method on a class with no
  subclasses anywhere in the program) catch ~70–85% of all devirtualization opportunities. The hard cases (type flows
  through multiple function calls, polymorphic generic code, maybe-sealed classes) catch the remaining 15–30% and
  require 4–5x as much code to get right.
  - Bounds check elimination: Simple patterns (for i in range(len(arr)): arr[i], explicit bounds-checked index) catch
  ~60–80%. The rest needs interprocedural range analysis.
  - Unboxing / range proofs on ints: "Only assigned from int literals and arithmetic on provably-bounded values" catches
   ~70% of loop counters, indices, and small accumulators. Cross-function inference catches the rest.
  - Escape analysis for stack allocation: "Doesn't leave this function, isn't stored in any container, isn't captured by
   a closure" catches ~50–70%. Full whole-program escape analysis catches more but is famously bug-prone.
  - Write barrier elision: Trivial patterns catch ~40–60% with almost no complexity.

  If you run the numbers, a "conservative first pass" analyzer that takes only the clear cases for each of these lands
  at roughly 60–75% of the peak performance a sophisticated analyzer would achieve, for something like 15–25% of the
  implementation complexity. That's an unusually good deal, and it's exactly the deal mypyc, early JIT compilers, and
  many other projects have taken.

  In absolute terms: conservative-analyzer version of your compiler probably hits 1.5–2.5x of C++ for typical code
  instead of 1.1–1.3x. You lose maybe a factor of 1.3-2x on the runtime, and you save something like 3–5x on
  implementation effort. For a project where the bottleneck is soundness and verifiability rather than
  benchmark-chasing, that's clearly worth it.

  Why this trade is particularly favorable for your situation

  Three things stack in your favor:

  1. Failure mode asymmetry. An unsound aggressive optimization produces silent wrong results — memory corruption, data
  being mutated from under you, exceptions appearing from code that shouldn't raise them. These are the worst bugs in
  all of software; they're frequently days-to-weeks to diagnose and they destroy user trust in the compiler. A
  conservative analyzer's failure mode is "slightly slower than it could be," which is completely recoverable and causes
   zero correctness problems. When you're building this through AI-assisted iteration and the human reviewer has to
  verify soundness by hand, you want the failure modes to be visible, not silent. Conservative analysis is vastly safer
  here.
  2. Annotations recover the lost ground where it matters. The 30–40% of performance you leave on the table isn't evenly
   distributed — most of it sits in a small number of hot functions per program, the same functions a user would profile
   and tune anyway. Give users explicit annotations (@sealed, @final, Int32 / Int64, @no_escape, @assume_in_range) and
  they can recover the missed optimizations at the exact spots that matter without the compiler needing to prove
  anything. The user asserts the fact, the compiler trusts it and runs the fast path. This is how Rust's unsafe, C++'s
  final and noexcept, and Cython's cdef all work. It's a proven pattern and it maps naturally onto your design — you
  already have explicit opt-in types for ints, you can extend the same principle everywhere else.
  3. Debuggability. A conservative analyzer can emit diagnostics: "couldn't optimize this call site because I couldn't
  prove X." The user reads the diagnostic, adds an annotation, and gets the fast path. An aggressive analyzer fails
  silently in the wrong direction — either the optimization works or it doesn't, and when it doesn't, you don't know
  why. For a compiler you're actively developing, "tell me why you bailed out" is enormously valuable, because it tells
  you exactly which analyses are worth strengthening next.

  The "ratchet" path

  The best part of this approach is that you don't have to pick a conservatism level once and commit. You ship version 1
   of the compiler with the simplest possible analyses — the "obvious yes" cases only. It's correct, it runs, it's maybe
   3–5x slower than the theoretical ceiling but still 8–15x faster than CPython, and you can trust it. Users start using
   it, file bug reports about correctness (which are all rare and fixable), and profile their code.

  From the profiling data, you see which analyses would pay off most — "if you devirtualized this one call pattern,
  users would get 2x on their workload." You implement that specific analysis strengthening, ship it, and gain a factor
  of performance with a bounded piece of new code. You repeat.

  Each step of the ratchet is testable in isolation (you can turn the new analysis on and off and compare results),
  recoverable (if the new analysis turns out to be buggy, you revert to the previous conservative version), and driven
  by real workloads (you're strengthening the analyses that actually pay off for real code, not the ones that look
  clever in theory). This is how basically every successful compiler project has incrementally grown its optimizer, and
  it avoids the failure mode where someone tries to build a perfect analyzer upfront, gets lost in corner cases, and
  ships nothing.

  For your situation specifically — AI-assisted build, human-reviewer bottleneck, hobby OS as the forcing function —
  this ratchet approach is close to optimal. It lets you start from a compiler you can fully reason about, and grow it
  in small verifiable increments whose cost/benefit you can evaluate at each step. You're never committed to a piece of
  sophisticated analysis that turned out to be wrong.

  The direct answer

  Yes, conservative bail-out is the right approach, and the tradeoff is strongly favorable for this project. You pay
  maybe a factor of 1.3–2x on peak runtime performance in exchange for a compiler that's 3–5x simpler to build,
  substantially safer against correctness bugs, and easier to grow incrementally. The performance cost is real but
  recoverable through user annotations at the specific spots where it matters, and the implementation savings free up
  effort to spend on the parts of the compiler where it actually counts — the runtime, the GC, the CPython compatibility
   layer, the codegen quality. It's the standard choice for production compilers for exactly the reasons you intuited,
  and nothing about your situation makes it a worse choice than usual. If anything, it makes it a better one.

> What's the minimum amount of flexibility that would have to be removed from the Python programming language in order
  to be able to statically compile it? Is it the same as what's removed from Cython, or something less?

● This is a genuinely interesting language-design question, and the answer is: considerably less than Cython removes,
  but more than people usually think, because the killers are a few specific features rather than "dynamic typing" as a
  whole.

  What actually blocks static compilation

  Static compilation — in the strict sense of "emit native code ahead of time with no embedded interpreter and no
  runtime type dispatch on every operation" — is blocked by a small set of features that defeat any attempt to know, at
  compile time, what an operation means:

  1. eval / exec / compile on arbitrary strings. These can introduce new names, new classes, new methods into any scope
  at runtime. As long as they exist, the compiler can never prove a name binding is final.
  2. Monkey-patching of classes and modules from the outside. SomeClass.method = other_fn, module.attr = x, replacing
  __class__, assigning to __dict__. These mean that even a fully-typed call obj.foo() can't be lowered to a direct call
  — someone may have swapped foo out.
  3. __getattr__ / __getattribute__ / __setattr__ as escape hatches. Any attribute access can run arbitrary Python.
  Attribute resolution is not a lookup, it's a computation.
  4. Metaclasses with custom __call__ / __instancecheck__ / __subclasscheck__. isinstance and type() can lie;
  MyClass(...) can return anything.
  5. Dynamic class creation via type(name, bases, dict) and dynamic base classes. The class hierarchy isn't knowable
  until runtime.
  6. First-class frames and stack introspection — sys._getframe, inspect.currentframe, reading/writing f_locals. These
  force the compiler to materialize a real frame object with a real locals dict, which defeats register allocation and
  kills most optimizations. (This is why PEP 667 matters.)
  7. Arbitrary __import__ hooks and import-time side effects. Imports can run code that mutates anything.
  8. globals() / locals() as mutable dicts you can write through. Name binding is no longer a compile-time concept.

  If you forbid those eight things, and you additionally require that names, once bound in a module or class body, are
  final (the single most important constraint — sometimes called "sealing"), then essentially everything else in Python
  — duck typing, **kwargs, generators, decorators, descriptors, multiple inheritance, exceptions, closures, async/await,
   even dynamic dispatch — is compilable. You just need a sufficiently smart compiler and, crucially, type inference or
  type annotations that the compiler is allowed to trust.

  Note what is not on this list: dynamic typing itself, duck typing, heterogeneous containers, None, big ints, **kwargs.
   These are all compilable with tagged unions, specialization, and boxing. They cost performance, not compilability.

  Is that the same as Cython?

  No — Cython removes much more, and for a different reason.

  Cython is not really "Python minus dynamism." It's "Python with an opt-in second type system (cdef) that bypasses
  Python's object model entirely." When you write plain Python in a Cython file, Cython doesn't statically compile it —
  it generates C code that calls into CPython's C API to do exactly the same things the interpreter would. The speedup
  only comes when you annotate with cdef int, cdef class, typed memoryviews, etc., at which point you are programming in
   a C-like sublanguage that happens to share syntax with Python.

  So Cython's constraints on the fast path are much stricter than what static compilation fundamentally requires:

  - cdef class can't be monkey-patched, can't have __dict__ unless you opt in, has a fixed layout and fixed method
  table.
  - cdef functions can't be introspected, overridden from Python, or decorated arbitrarily.
  - Typed variables can't hold None unless declared as object or nullable.
  - Exceptions across C boundaries need explicit except clauses to propagate.

  The reason Cython is so restrictive is that it isn't trying to compile Python — it's trying to let you write C while
  looking like Python, and then freely mix the two. The restrictions are what make the C/Python boundary efficient.

  The middle ground — what "statically compilable Python" actually looks like

  The projects that have tried to honestly statically compile real Python — Shed Skin, Nuitka (in its "C level" mode),
  mypyc, Mojo, and to a lesser extent Pyston and Pyjion — all converge on roughly the same constraint set:

  - Sealed modules and classes. No runtime addition of names or methods. This is the big one. mypyc calls it "native
  classes"; Mojo calls it struct vs class.
  - No eval/exec on compiled code paths. You can still call out to the interpreter for these.
  - No arbitrary monkey-patching of compiled classes from outside. Other compiled code sees the sealed view.
  - Types must be inferable or annotated, and the compiler must be allowed to trust the annotations rather than
  re-checking at runtime.
  - No stack-frame introspection inside hot code.

  Everything else — duck typing, **kwargs, generators, closures, exceptions, multiple inheritance, descriptors,
  isinstance, even __getattr__ on non-compiled classes — can be preserved. mypyc is the cleanest demonstration of this:
  it compiles a very large, almost unrestricted subset of Python to C and gets real speedups, and the compilation unit
  is "a module whose classes you agree are sealed."

  The one project that has most aggressively tried to answer your exact question is Mojo, which is explicitly framed as
  "what if Python had a strict mode where you said fn instead of def and struct instead of class, and those constructs
  were statically compiled while the rest of Python still worked normally?" Mojo's answer is basically the list above:
  sealed types, no monkey-patching of structs, trusted annotations, no frame introspection — but it keeps generators,
  exceptions, overloading, traits, and the full expression language.

  Short answer

  The minimum you have to give up is:

  1. eval/exec/compile on live scopes,
  2. runtime mutation of module and class namespaces (monkey-patching),
  3. stack-frame introspection,
  4. writable globals()/locals(),
  5. the ability for __getattr__/metaclasses on compiled classes to intercept ordinary lookups,

  plus the meta-constraint that annotations, once written, are trusted and not re-checked.

  That is substantially less than what Cython's fast path gives up, because Cython additionally restricts layout,
  nullability, method dispatch, exception propagation, and the entire notion of "is this a Python object or a C value" —
   restrictions that come from its C-interop goal, not from the compilation goal itself. mypyc and Mojo are closer to
  the true minimum.

✻ Churned for 46s

> Regarding (2), what if the compilation stage tracked every attribute every dynmically added to an object and
  automatically included that attribute as a part of the class? We just couldn't allow it for non-literals (i.e.,
  variables) being used as attribute names (i.e., in `setattr`), and even then maybe we could as long as we can track
  that the variables never change after initialization, and even in the case where we can't tracking that, we could
  still have objects include an internal dictionary of attribute:value pairs in which we look up string attribute names
   for getting and setting values for the cases where we couldn't resolve the attribute name at compile time. We could
  do something similar for the case of `SomeClass.method = other_fn` and `SomeClass.attribute = some_value`, except
  that in the case where our (simple) compile-time tracking can't prove that SomeClass.method is set once and only
  once, we'd just have to have the class's value for `method` be a pointer to a function rather than a direct function,
   and of course have it as a part of our design that classes have two or maybe four types of functions: direct
  function, pointer to a function, function_name:function dictionary entry, and function_name:function_ptr dictionary
  entry.

  Regarding (1), what if we just treated any new binding created by these commands as non-literal, untrackable names
  that we then use our objects' and classes' internal attribute:value dictionaries for? But I think the bigger problem
  with these commands is that they can't work unless we embed the entire actual compiler (or, alternatively, a separate
   interpreter) into the compiled program. Which is doable, but it would be ridiculously slow in the embedded compiler
  case, and would require an entirely separate implementation of the whole language just for these functions in the
  embedded interpreter case.

  I can't see a way around 4.

  Regarding (5), maybe that could work if we just make all attributes of such a class the attribute:value or
  function_name:function_ptr dictionary entry type, and only allow it when we can track the name of the class being
  generated. But we don't have to do the `make all attributes key:value types` trick if we can track `dict`'s contents
  at compile time, or if we can only track its keys, then we only need function_ptrs instead of direct functions, and
  if we can only track its values, then we only need attribute lookups but not function pointers. And we don't have to
  limit it to the case where we can track the name of the generated class at compile time if we just use a top-level
  key:value lookup at runtime and use it for all class name references that we don't recognize at compile time.

  Except that I'm not sure if we could honor `bases` just using dictionary look ups in the case where we can't track
  the value of `bases` at compile time for a dynamic class hierarchy. Maybe it could work if we had a secondary path
  for class inheritance that evaluates the whole hierarchy of bases using attribute lookups at runtime for each
  instantiation of the class, function override, etc., or maybe we wouldn't have to traverse the whole hierarchy each
  time if we used some type of caching?

  Regarding (7), I don't see the particular problem, since import hooks and import-time side effects can't do any type
  of mutation that we couldn't already do in general, outside of import hooks and imports.

  Regarding (8), we could still allow use of globals() and locals() but just not allow one to delete keys from them. In
   the case of adding a key, it just gets added to the aforementioned top-level key:value lookup and any use of that
  key will go through the top-level lookup since the key won't be recognized at compile time. Deleting a key from
  globals() or locals() could also work if we only allow deleting keys that had been dynamically added in the first
  place.

  A lot of the above tricks would slow down execution, but the user could avoid each slowdown just by not using certain
   untrackable dynamic features, or alternatively setting project flags that categorically don't allow them so he/she
  doesn't have to guess, and either way, I think the slowdowns would still not be anything close to the slowdown of
  using CPython or probably even PyPy.




● You're basically reinventing, from first principles, the design that real high-performance Python implementations
  converge on — which is a strong signal your instincts are right. Let me go through where I think you're correct, where
   the proposal is stronger than I gave it credit for, and where there are subtleties worth naming.

  (2) — your proposal is essentially how V8, PyPy, and mypyc work

  The "direct slot / function pointer / name-keyed dict / name-keyed pointer dict" hierarchy is exactly the classical
  inline-cache design. V8 calls the layout part "hidden classes with transitions," Self called them "maps," PyPy calls
  them "map-transitions." The dispatch part is "monomorphic / polymorphic / megamorphic inline caches." You've
  independently described the same four-tier structure that decades of dynamic-language implementation converged on.

  The one thing this design runs into that's worth flagging: the tension between separate compilation and whole-program
  specialization. If foo.py defines class C and bar.py does C.method = other, then compiling foo.py in isolation can't
  know whether C.method should be a direct call or an indirect call — the information lives in another module. Your
  options are:

  - Closed-world / whole-program compilation. See the whole program at once, specialize aggressively. This is what Shed
  Skin and mypyc effectively require.
  - Conservatively pessimize at module boundaries. Every externally-visible attribute is a pointer or dict entry; only
  intra-module calls can be devirtualized.
  - Link-time specialization. Emit multiple versions and pick at link time. Expensive, rarely done.

  None of these defeat your design — they just mean the "tracking" you described is actually a whole-program analysis,
  not a per-module one, if you want the fast path to fire often. That's a practical constraint that shapes the toolchain
   (you can't just python -c "import foo" anymore; you need a build step).

  (1) — you're right about the fallback dict, and partly right about the embedding cost

  Routing eval/exec results through the fallback name dict is fine for correctness. The real cost isn't that binding —
  it's that every global access after an eval call has to assume the eval could have written anything, so the compiler
  can't turn any global reference into a direct memory load across an eval. In practice, if eval is possible anywhere,
  all global accesses become dict lookups, which is where CPython already is.

  On embedding: you're right that it's a nuisance, but the costs are more bearable than you imply. CPython's bytecode
  interpreter core is ~20k lines of C, and compiled programs that want eval could just link it as a library. It doesn't
  have to be a "reasonably fast" interpreter — it just has to produce correct results on the slow path. Plenty of static
   languages embed an interpreter for their metaprogramming tier (Julia, D, Zig comptime in the other direction). The
  maintenance cost of two language implementations is real but not exotic.

  A cleaner middle path you didn't mention: restrict eval/exec to compile-time only — treat them like a macro system.
  Any eval call whose argument is a compile-time-known string gets evaluated during compilation and inlined; runtime
  eval is a separate opt-in. Mojo and Zig both pick some version of this. It eliminates the embedding problem in 90% of
  real programs while keeping the semantics in the remaining 10%.

  (4) — there actually is a way around it, via deopt metadata

  I was too pessimistic here. Production JITs (V8, HotSpot, LuaJIT) handle frame introspection via deoptimization
  tables: the compiler emits side tables mapping every safepoint PC in the machine code back to the source-level
  variable locations (which register, which stack slot, or "constant folded, value is X"). When someone calls
  sys._getframe(), the runtime walks the stack, finds the table entry for the current PC, and reconstructs a synthetic
  frame object from the machine state.

  This is completely viable for AOT compilation too. The costs are:
  - Compile-time bookkeeping (non-trivial but manageable)
  - Binary-size overhead for the side tables (can be 10–30% of code size; compressible)
  - A small penalty at safepoints because the compiler has to keep values "materializable" even when it doesn't need
  them

  So (4) is solvable. The reason I listed it as a blocker is that it's the feature that causes the most pervasive
  pessimization if you handle it naively, and the infrastructure to handle it properly is substantial — you essentially
