  need the same machinery a JIT uses for deopt. For a "small statically-compiled Python" project, it's the feature most
  likely to get dropped. For a serious one, it's solvable.

  (5) — mostly right, with one subtlety

  Your dict-based metaclass dispatch is correct and workable. The caching-MRO point is also right: CPython already
  computes C3 linearization once per class and caches it, so dynamic bases doesn't cost per-call, only
  per-class-construction.

  The subtlety I'd add: the worst offender in metaclass-land isn't bases or MRO, it's __call__. MyClass(...) desugars to
   type(MyClass).__call__(MyClass, ...), and that can return any object of any type. So even code like x = MyClass();
  x.foo() can't statically know what x is. You can handle this the same way you'd handle any other polymorphic call site
   — inline cache on the return type of __call__ — but it does mean "construction" isn't a statically-typed operation
  even for statically-declared classes, unless you forbid __call__ overrides on metaclasses. mypyc forbids them for
  native classes. Mojo sidesteps it by making struct instantiation a different syntactic operation than class
  instantiation.

  Also: __instancecheck__ / __subclasscheck__ mean isinstance is never a direct type-tag comparison in the general case.
   Compiled code has to call out to the metaclass hook. It's correct, just not free.

  (7) — you're right, I was wrong

  Imports are just code execution, and if you've handled (1) and (2) you've handled imports. The ordering concern is no
  different from any other program-order concern. I was padding the list; (7) should be collapsed into (2).

  (8) — works with PEP 667 semantics, but only with them

  Your "dynamically-added-only deletes" idea is basically PEP 667's design. Pre-PEP-667, locals() had semantics that
  were genuinely incompatible with static compilation of fast locals — writes through the dict could be either visible
  or invisible to the function, depending on whether a LOAD_FAST intervened, and exec(..., locals()) had
  observable-but-not-always-visible effects. The semantics were so weird that CPython itself couldn't implement them
  consistently.

  PEP 667 fixed this by making locals() return a write-through proxy with well-defined semantics: reads see the current
  fast locals, writes go into an overlay dict that the function itself never reads. This lets the compiler
  register-allocate fast locals freely, because from the function's own perspective nothing ever writes to them from
  outside, while locals() still appears to work for external code. Your design is the same shape. So with modern (3.13+)
   Python semantics, (8) is solvable; against pre-3.13 semantics, it isn't really.

  globals() is easier and always has been — module globals are already dict-backed, so globals() just exposes that dict,
   and the compiler's job is to decide when it's allowed to cache a global reference across a potential dict mutation.

  The meta-point

  When you put all of this together, what you've described is: an AOT compiler that specializes aggressively where the
  whole-program view permits, and falls back to inline-cache-backed dict lookups otherwise, with deopt metadata for
  introspection and a write-through proxy for locals. That is a real, shippable design, and it's the design that mypyc,
  Mojo, and (in JIT form) PyPy all converge on. Your intuition that "the slowdowns would still be much better than
  CPython or probably PyPy" is correct for the fast path — mypyc routinely gets 2–5x over CPython on type-annotated code
   without any JIT, purely from AOT specialization. For the slow path (megamorphic calls, heavy metaclass use,
  eval-riddled code) you end up roughly at CPython speed, which is the floor: you can't go slower than "do what CPython
  does" because that's what you're literally doing.

  The part that's genuinely hard, and that isn't a language-design question but an engineering one, is the binary size
  and compile time cost of the bookkeeping. Every dynamic site needs metadata — inline caches, deopt tables, fallback
  dicts, per-class method tables with slots for every attribute ever assigned anywhere in the program. For a large
  Python program (Django, say), the tables get big. That's the main reason nobody has shipped a "compile all of real
  Python to a native binary" tool even though the theory has been clear for twenty years — not that it's impossible, but
   that the cost/benefit vs. "just run PyPy" has never been compelling. Mojo is the current serious attempt, and its bet
   is essentially that a slightly restricted Python (fn/struct for the fast path, def/class for the slow path) hits a
  sweet spot your proposal is circling around.

✻ Crunched for 1m 53s

> So, you're basically saying that a mypyc or mojo compiler does pretty much everything I proposed, and the speed
  increase for the case in which everything in the program takes the fast paths is only about 2-5x over Cpython, which
  isn't even as fast as PyPy? Or do mypyc or mojo not do as much as my recommendation, or were you talking about the
  slow paths and not the fast paths? I think in a couple of places you compared these optimizations to optimizations
  CPython or V8 makes, but I don't think that's really comparable as far as execution speedup goes because there's a
  huge difference between statically compiling a program and using a VM/bytecode dispatch loop. Regarding the speedup
  increases you cited without JIT, I wonder how much faster these ideas could be if we just compiled to LLVM and got
  JIT for free. Regarding all the metadata you mentioned that every dynamic site needs, two things. 1., this only
  applies to dynamic sites, by which I guess you mean compile-time objects that the compiler is unable to make without
  implementing dynamic tricks for, and 2., even for those objects, we only implement the specific tables, indirections,
   etc. that we need to depending on its level of dynamicism/unpredictabiility. So, for a typical simple Python program
   that doesn't use a lot of magic, it seems that the extra memory usage would be negligible.

● Good pushback. I was being loose in a couple of places and you caught it.

  The 2–5x is mypyc-specific, not the ceiling

  mypyc's ceiling is that low because of deliberate design choices, not because it's implementing your proposal fully.
  Specifically:

  1. mypyc still uses CPython's object model. A mypyc-compiled int is still a PyObject* with a refcount header and a
  type pointer. A list is still CPython's list. Strings are still CPython's strings. This is intentional — mypyc's goal
  is seamless interop with unmodified CPython extensions, which means every object crossing a module boundary has to
  look like a CPython object. That alone costs you 3–10x on anything arithmetic-heavy, because you're not unboxing.
  2. mypyc uses reference counting, because CPython does, for the same interop reason. Refcount traffic is one of the
  biggest single costs in CPython, and mypyc inherits it.
  3. mypyc is not whole-program. Each module compiles independently. It devirtualizes intra-module calls and attribute
  accesses on "native classes" it can see, but anything crossing a module boundary goes through the regular Python call
  path.
  4. mypyc does almost no classical optimization. It lowers to C, and relies on the C compiler's optimizer. It doesn't
  do escape analysis, doesn't stack-allocate, doesn't unbox, doesn't inline across .c files.

  So mypyc's 2–5x is "what you get when you specialize dispatch but keep CPython's data representation." Your proposal,
  implemented without those interop constraints, would do dramatically better — because the biggest wins in
  dynamic-language compilation come from changing the object representation, not from specializing dispatch. Unboxing an
   int into an actual machine integer is worth more than devirtualizing ten method calls.

  The realistic ceiling for "your proposal, implemented seriously, AOT, with its own object model and no CPython-compat
  constraint" is more like 10–50x over CPython for typical code and 100–1000x for numeric / tight-loop code. Shed Skin
  (whole-program type inference, restricted Python, its own object model) gets to roughly that range on the benchmarks
  it handles. Cython with full cdef annotations gets C speed — same ballpark, same reason: it stops using CPython's
  object model. Mojo's published numbers for numeric kernels are in the thousands-of-x range vs CPython; for general
  code it's hard to say because there aren't good published general-purpose benchmarks yet, but the design is consistent
   with 10–30x on the fast path.

  And you're right about the CPython/V8 comparison not being apples-to-apples. When I pointed at V8 as evidence that the
   design works, I meant "V8 demonstrates that the dispatch structure you're proposing is sound" — not "V8's absolute
  performance is the ceiling." V8 is JIT'd and has to pay dispatch overhead; an AOT compiler with the same dispatch
  structure and LLVM's backend should be faster on steady-state code, because it can spend compile-time budget that a
  JIT can't afford.

  LLVM: better than "free JIT," not worse

  Your instinct here is right but the framing slightly understates it. You don't want LLVM for the JIT — you want LLVM
  because AOT through LLVM is strictly better than JIT when you already know the types statically.

  A JIT's unique capability is runtime type feedback: it observes what types actually flow through a site and
  specializes. That's useful only when static analysis can't figure the types out. If your compile-time tracking has
  already proven that x.foo() is monomorphic and dispatches to C.foo, a JIT gives you nothing over an AOT compiler — you
   both emit the same direct call. But the AOT compiler can spend 10 seconds on inlining, loop unrolling, vectorization,
   and global value numbering at that site, while a JIT has maybe a millisecond before the user notices warmup.

  So the design is:

  - Your analysis figures out what's statically knowable (which is most of most programs).
  - LLVM does the heavy lifting on the code generated from the knowable parts: SSA, inlining, LICM, vectorization,
  register allocation. All the classical optimizations you'd get from a C compiler.
  - Inline caches and dict fallbacks handle the residual dynamic sites, and LLVM still optimizes the fast path through
  them.
  - No runtime JIT at all — the binary is just a binary.

  This is what Julia does (LLVM, AOT-ish via precompilation, type inference), and it's why Julia reaches C speed on
  numeric code despite being a dynamic language with multiple dispatch. It's the model that fits your proposal.

  The one thing you give up vs. a JIT is the ability to recover from a wrong static guess cheaply. A JIT can speculate
  aggressively and deopt; an AOT compiler has to be conservative at sites where it's uncertain. But since your proposal
  already has a well-defined fallback (dict lookup), that's fine — the uncertain sites just go to the slow path and stay
   there.

  Metadata overhead: you're right, I was overstating

  Rereading what I wrote, I was describing worst case as if it were typical. You're right that the overhead is
  pay-for-what-you-use, and a plain Python program — one that defines classes, uses them, passes them around, doesn't
  monkey-patch, doesn't eval, doesn't introspect frames — pays essentially nothing beyond what a statically-typed
  language would pay.

  Concretely, for a simple program:
  - Sealed, monomorphic classes: just a C struct layout plus a vtable. Same cost as C++.
  - Call sites proven monomorphic: direct calls. Zero overhead.
  - No sys._getframe: no deopt metadata. Zero overhead.
  - No eval/exec: no embedded interpreter. Zero overhead.
  - No writable locals(): locals stay in registers. Zero overhead.
  - Fallback dict for untrackable globals: exists, but if the compiler proves nothing untrackable happens, it's never
  populated and can be omitted.

  The overhead scales with how much dynamism you actually use. A program that uses a lot of getattr(obj, some_variable)
  pays for that with a dict fallback on those objects, but a program that doesn't use it at all pays nothing. My
  "Django" example was misleading — Django actually does a lot of metaclass and descriptor work, so it would land on the
   "pay for what you use, and you use a lot" end, but that's a property of Django, not of Python as a language. A
  numeric script or a CLI tool would have negligible overhead.

  Where the metadata actually does get large is deopt tables for introspection (because they're per-safepoint, not
  per-dynamic-site) and inline cache entries for megamorphic call sites (because they grow with observed type variety).
  But again, these only exist if the relevant features are used. A program with no introspection has no deopt tables.

  The honest summary

  If someone actually built your proposal — whole-program analysis, tiered dispatch (direct / vtable / dict), own object
   model with unboxing, LLVM backend, PEP 667 locals(), optional embedded interpreter for eval — for typical annotated
  Python, I'd expect:

  - 10–30x over CPython on general code
  - 50–500x on numeric / tight-loop code (matching Cython/Julia territory)
  - Roughly on par with or faster than PyPy on steady-state code, because LLVM's AOT optimization budget beats a tracing
   JIT's per-trace budget
  - Slower than PyPy on highly polymorphic code where runtime type feedback catches patterns static analysis misses
  - Binary sizes maybe 1.5–3x the source size for typical code, more if heavy metaprogramming is used
  - Compile times substantially worse than CPython (LLVM is slow), comparable to a C++ project of similar size

  The reason nobody has shipped this isn't that it's impossible — it's that the engineering budget to build it is
  enormous (mypyc is ~8 years of work for a much more conservative design, Mojo has a small team and years of runway),
  and the payoff is strongest exactly in the domain where people already use NumPy, Cython, or write a C extension. The
  "compile all your Python to fast native code" market keeps turning out to be smaller than expected because the tail of
   "Python that does weird things" is long, and the head of "Python that does numeric things" is already served.

  Mojo is the current serious attempt, and its explicit bet is that your design is correct but needs to be packaged as a
   slightly-different language (with fn/struct as an opt-in fast-path tier) to make the engineering tractable and the
  performance model legible to users. Whether that bet pays off is an open question — but the technical design you're
  describing is, as far as I can tell, basically right.

✻ Worked for 1m 33s

> Could we just transpile to C or C++ instead of compiling to LLVM and get faster code? Does LLVM produce a bytecode
  interpreter or a static runtime?

● Two separate questions, and the second one clears up a misconception that's worth addressing first.

  LLVM is not a bytecode interpreter

  LLVM IR is sometimes called "bytecode" and there's even an lli tool that interprets it, but nobody uses that in
  production — it exists for testing LLVM itself. When you "compile to LLVM," what actually happens is:

  1. Your frontend emits LLVM IR (a typed SSA intermediate representation).
  2. LLVM's optimizer runs passes on the IR (inlining, GVN, LICM, vectorization, register allocation, etc.).
  3. LLVM's backend lowers the IR to native machine code for the target architecture.
  4. You get a regular native executable, statically linked to whatever runtime you provide.

  That's the same pipeline Clang uses. Clang is a C/C++ frontend whose only job is to produce LLVM IR; from there, C
  code and your Python-compiler's output go through identical optimization and codegen. There is no runtime IR
  interpretation, no bytecode dispatch loop, no LLVM-imposed runtime. The runtime is whatever you link in — libc, your
  GC, your fallback-dict machinery — LLVM doesn't care.

  (LLVM does have an optional JIT component called ORC, used by Julia and others, but that's a separate library you
  explicitly opt into. AOT compilation through LLVM has no JIT anywhere in sight.)

  Transpiling to C is a real option and some fast languages do it

  Nim, Vala, Chicken Scheme, Cython, and several others transpile to C and get genuinely fast code. Nim in particular is
   competitive with hand-written C on many benchmarks. So "C as a portable assembler" is a valid strategy — it's not
  obviously slower than LLVM direct, and on some workloads GCC's backend beats LLVM's by a few percent.

  The C backend has some real advantages:

  - Portability. Any platform with a C compiler can host your language. LLVM doesn't support every exotic target.
  - Debugging. Readable intermediate output helps when your compiler is misbehaving.
  - Mature optimizers. GCC has been tuned on real workloads for 35+ years.
  - No LLVM dependency. LLVM is ~100MB of build dependency and slow to link against.
  - Free choice of backend. You can use GCC, Clang, MSVC, ICC, whichever is best for the target.

  Nim's existence proves that for languages in the same neighborhood as C (manual memory management or simple GC, static
   types, no exotic dispatch), transpiling to C loses essentially nothing.

  But for a language like Python, LLVM direct is usually slightly better

  The argument against C transpilation isn't that C compilers are worse than LLVM — they're roughly on par. The argument
   is that C can't express some things your compiler wants to tell the backend, and those things matter more for
  Python-like languages than they do for Nim.

  Concretely:

  1. Precise aliasing information. When you've statically proven that two pointers don't alias, LLVM lets you annotate
  that with noalias metadata, and the optimizer uses it for reordering, hoisting, and vectorization. In C you have
  restrict, which is clunky, applies only to function parameters, and doesn't scale to complex aggregate types. When you
   lower a Python list-of-ints to C, you drop everything the type system proved about it.

  2. GC integration. A compiler for a GC'd language needs to tell the runtime, at every safepoint, which machine
  registers and stack slots hold live pointers. LLVM has gc.statepoint and gc.relocate intrinsics for exactly this, and
  emits precise stack maps. In C, there is no portable way to express it — you'd have to fall back to conservative stack
   scanning (slower, less precise) or shadow stacks (slow overhead on every function). If you want a precise moving GC,
  LLVM direct is basically the only realistic option.

  3. Tail calls. Some compilation strategies (continuation-passing, trampolined interpreters, certain functional-style
  patterns) need guaranteed tail call optimization. LLVM has a musttail attribute that guarantees it. C doesn't — GCC
  and Clang usually do TCO at -O2, but you can't rely on it, and you can't force it. Chicken Scheme works around this
  with an elaborate trampolining scheme that costs performance.

  4. Custom calling conventions. LLVM exposes several calling conventions (fastcc, coldcc, swiftcc, etc.) and lets you
  define your own for specific uses like "this call is from JIT'd code into the runtime." C gives you exactly one
  convention per target plus whatever extensions your compiler offers.

  5. SIMD and intrinsics. Portable. LLVM has target-independent vector types. C has per-compiler intrinsic headers that
  you pick from at code-gen time.

  6. Exception handling. LLVM has first-class support for zero-cost exception handling via landingpad/invoke and
  funclets on Windows. In C you'd use setjmp/longjmp (slow because every try block has a runtime cost) or
  compiler-specific extensions like __try on MSVC. For a language where exceptions are on the normal control-flow path
  (and in Python, StopIteration really is on a hot path), this matters.

  7. Precise float control, fences, atomics. LLVM exposes all of this uniformly. C got <stdatomic.h> eventually but it's
   newer and less flexible.

  8. Inline assembly and intrinsics for the unusual cases. Both C and LLVM have this, but LLVM's is more structured and
  survives optimization better.

  The effect of all this is that transpiling to C forces you to either drop information (and get slightly-worse codegen
  because the C compiler can't reason about what you knew) or encode workarounds (shadow stacks for GC, setjmp for
  exceptions, trampolines for tail calls) that cost performance at runtime. LLVM lets you preserve the information
  through to codegen.

  The gap is small, but the engineering cost favors LLVM anyway

  On pure benchmark numbers, "your language through C" vs. "your language through LLVM IR" is typically within 5–15% of
  each other, and which side wins depends on the workload. That's the honest comparison. For a Python compiler
  specifically, the edge tilts toward LLVM because of GC integration and exception handling — features that C expresses
  only awkwardly and where the workarounds cost more than 5–15%.

  The bigger factor than "C vs LLVM" is how much type and aliasing information your compiler's own IR preserves before
  it hits whichever backend. A sloppy frontend that throws away type info early will get equally mediocre code from both
   backends. A careful frontend that tracks precise types and aliasing will get good code from both — but only LLVM lets
   you forward all of that information to the final stage.

  There's also a compile-time argument for LLVM: direct IR generation is typically faster end-to-end than transpiling to
   C and then parsing the C back in. Two parse+lower passes is slower than one, and C parsing is surprisingly expensive.

  The historical pattern is clear: newer systems languages (Rust, Swift, Julia, Crystal, Zig until recently) all picked
  LLVM direct. Older ones (Nim, Vala, Chicken, early Haxe) picked C. Nobody who has built a language on top of C
  transpilation has reported being slower than LLVM as a result — they've reported being slower because of other design
  decisions, or roughly equivalent. So C is viable; it's just not usually the right pick for a language that wants to do
   anything C doesn't natively support, and a Python compiler that's doing accurate GC and real exception handling is
  firmly in that camp.

✻ Churned for 1m 27s

> What I still don't understand is, I think you said that Cython's speed is basically identical to native C? And all
  Cython really adds is static types, and takes away the dynamic stuff. My compiler could easily just infer static
  types instead of making the user enter them, for most of most Python programs (ones that aren't very magical). So,
  why wouldn't my compiler also achieve basically the speed of native C?

● This is the sharpest version of the question and the honest answer is the one I've been dancing around: Cython's C
  speed does not come from adding types. It comes from letting you opt into C semantics. Type inference gives you the
  types, but it doesn't give you the semantics, and the semantic gap is where the remaining slowdown lives.

  Consider what cdef int x actually means in Cython. It doesn't mean "a Python int that the compiler knows is an int."
  It means "a C int." Specifically:

  - It's a 32-bit (or 64-bit) machine integer. It silently overflows. Python's int is arbitrary precision — 2**100
  works.
  - It can't be None. Python's int variable can be rebound to None at any time.
  - It can't be a subclass with overridden __add__. Python's int can.
  - It doesn't participate in __radd__ lookup against the other operand. Python's + operator does.
  - Division by zero is undefined behavior, not a ZeroDivisionError.
  - It has no refcount, no type pointer, no object header.

  When you write cdef int x = 5; cdef int y = x + 3, Cython emits a literal C int x = 5; int y = x + 3;. That's one
  machine instruction. It's fast because it's not Python anymore — it's C wearing a Python costume.

  Now consider what your proposed compiler has to emit for x = 5; y = x + 3 in a plain Python function, even after
  perfect type inference has told it "x is always an int":

  1. Overflow. If your compiler uses a 64-bit machine int, then 2**62 + 2**62 gives the wrong answer — it has silently
  changed Python semantics. To preserve semantics you either (a) always use arbitrary-precision bigints (5–10x slower
  than C), (b) use a tagged representation where small values are unboxed and large ones heap-allocate (every arithmetic
   op has an overflow check and a fallback — ~20–40% overhead vs raw C), or (c) declare "we're just going to overflow,
  sorry" — at which point you have Cython's cdef int, and you've silently changed the language.
  2. Subclass dispatch. 3 + x in Python calls int.__add__(3, x), but if x is an instance of a subclass of int with
  __radd__ defined, Python actually calls x.__radd__(3) first. Static analysis can prove no such subclass exists only
  under a closed-world assumption — the whole program is known at compile time, no late loading, no eval, no C
  extensions creating subclasses. Under closed-world, you devirtualize. Without it, you need a type check.
  3. None-ability. If flow analysis proves the variable is never None on this path, great. If it can't, every use is
  preceded by a null check. C has no such check.
  4. Exceptions. a / b in Python can raise ZeroDivisionError. Even if you've proven a and b are both ints, you need
  either a zero check before every divide or a signal-handler-based trap. C doesn't do this.
  5. Reference semantics. x = y for objects is a pointer copy with a refcount bump (or a GC write barrier). x = y for C
  ints is a register copy. Even with perfect type info, if your int is heap-allocated (because it might overflow),
  you're still paying memory-allocator and refcount/GC costs that C doesn't pay.
  6. Mutation + aliasing. Python's list is a heap object with a length field, capacity field, element array, refcount,
  type pointer. Indexing a list does a bounds check and a type check on the result (because list elements are object,
  not int). C arrays have none of that. Even with "this is a list of int" inferred, you're paying for the indirection
  and the bounds check unless you can prove the index is in range (sometimes doable, often not).

  Each of these is a small constant factor. Stacked together on a tight numeric loop, they typically sum to a 1.5–3x
  slowdown vs hand-written C for integer-heavy code, even with perfect type inference and aggressive optimization. For
  float-heavy code the story is better — IEEE 754 semantics are the same in Python and C, so a float doesn't have the
  overflow problem, and you can get to essentially C speed. For code that's dominated by list/dict operations, you're
  stuck paying Python's container semantics.

  Julia is the clean data point

  Julia is the language that actually answers your question empirically. Julia is:
  - Dynamically typed at the source level
  - Type-inferred aggressively at compile time
  - Compiled through LLVM
  - Designed for numerical computing

  And Julia genuinely does hit C speed on numeric code. Not "close to" — actually matches or beats. So your intuition
  that type inference + LLVM should give you C speed is correct in principle.

  The catch is what Julia gave up to get there. Julia's Int silently overflows — it's a machine int by design. Julia's
  language spec explicitly says so. Julia decided, as a language-design decision, that arbitrary-precision integers by
  default were not worth the performance cost, and if you want them you write BigInt. This is the single most important
  design choice Julia made to get C-speed numeric code, and it's a choice Python can't make without changing the
  language.

  Julia also has no subclass-based __radd__ dispatch — it uses multiple dispatch with a fixed type lattice, which
  devirtualizes cleanly. Julia has no None on typed slots. Julia's arrays are typed at construction. Julia has
  exceptions but they're rare enough on the hot path that the compiler optimizes assuming they won't fire.

  So Julia is a proof that "dynamic-looking language + type inference + LLVM = C speed" is achievable, but it's also a
  proof that the design decisions you need to make to get there are language-level decisions, not compiler-level
  decisions. Julia picked different semantics than Python.

  The honest answer to your question

  If your compiler preserves Python semantics completely — arbitrary-precision ints, full subclass dispatch,
  None-ability, exception safety, container bounds/type checks — then the ceiling for typical integer code is somewhere
  around 1.5–3x slower than C, not C speed. For float-heavy code the ceiling is closer to C speed, maybe 1.1–1.3x. For
  code dominated by container operations it's worse, 2–5x. This is the Python-semantics tax, and no amount of type
  inference removes it.

  If your compiler is willing to quietly relax Python semantics in the places where the user "probably doesn't care" —
  assume no int overflow, assume no int subclasses, assume no None, assume no exceptions — then yes, you can hit C speed
   for typical code, and you'd essentially be reinventing Cython's fast path (or Mojo's fn/struct path, or Shed Skin's
  restricted subset). But at that point you've built a language whose semantics subtly diverge from Python's, and users
  will occasionally hit surprising bugs where 2**100 gives the wrong answer or a subclass doesn't get its overrides
  honored.

  This is exactly the tradeoff Cython makes explicit with cdef. When you write cdef int, you're signing a waiver: "I
  understand this is no longer Python semantics, I want C speed more than I want correctness on edge cases." The genius
  of Cython's design isn't the types — it's making the user explicitly choose which semantics they want, on a
  per-variable basis, so nobody gets surprised.

  Your compiler could absolutely do the same thing automatically for cases where it can prove the semantic divergence
  doesn't matter — prove via range analysis that no overflow is possible, prove via closed-world that no subclass
  exists, prove via flow analysis that None is impossible, prove via pattern matching that division is safe. Every proof
   you complete unlocks a C-speed code path. Every proof you can't complete costs you a fallback. For simple non-magical
   Python, you'd complete most of the proofs most of the time, and the generated code would be close to C speed — call
  it within 1.5x on average, hitting C speed on the subset where all the proofs go through, somewhat worse on the rest.
  That's a massive improvement over CPython (10–30x) and competitive with or better than PyPy, but it's not literally C.

  The remaining gap — the 1.5x you can't close — is the price of staying Python instead of becoming Cython. Whether that
   price is worth paying is the language-design question Mojo is currently betting its existence on.

✻ Brewed for 1m 42s

> If my compiler did all of those automatic proof cases you mentioned, and we also made the single compromise that
  numbers are ints by default and the user has to explicitly call for a BigInt, how much of an improvement would that
  be?

● This single compromise is disproportionately valuable — it's worth more than all the other proofs combined for numeric
   code, because the overflow-check tax isn't just the cost of the check itself. It's a cascade.

  Why the compromise unlocks more than it appears to

  When Python ints can overflow, your compiler is forced to represent them as tagged values — typically a word where the
   low bit distinguishes "small int stored inline" from "pointer to heap bigint." Every arithmetic operation has to:

  1. Check both operand tags.
  2. Do the machine arithmetic.
  3. Check for overflow.
  4. If overflow, branch to a slow path that heap-allocates a bigint.
  5. Re-tag the result.

  That sounds like it's maybe 3-5x overhead on an add, which is bad but bounded. But the real cost is what it prevents:

  - Register allocation. A tagged int has to be untagged before arithmetic and re-tagged after, so it can't stay in a
  register across a loop. A machine int can.
  - SIMD vectorization. LLVM can't vectorize a loop whose body contains an overflow-check branch and a bigint fallback.
  The whole loop falls out of the autovectorizer. With machine ints, the same loop vectorizes to AVX2/AVX-512 and runs
  4–16x faster than the scalar version.
  - Dense array storage. A list[int] with overflow-capable ints has to store either boxed PyObject* pointers or tagged
  values. Either way, iterating the list is pointer-chasing with cache misses every element. With machine ints,
  list[int] stores as a contiguous int64[], and iteration is sequential memory reads at memory-bandwidth speed — often
  5–20x faster than the boxed version on realistic working sets.
  - Loop optimizations. LICM, strength reduction, induction-variable analysis, loop unrolling — all of these get much
  more effective when the loop body is clean machine arithmetic without tag-check branches muddying the control flow
  graph.
  - Inlining budget. The bigint fallback path, even when never taken, counts against LLVM's inlining cost model.
  Functions stop being inlined because they "look expensive." With the fallback gone, small functions inline freely.

  So removing the overflow check doesn't save 30% — it uncaps a bunch of optimizations that were stuck behind it.

  Rough quantitative estimate

  Starting from the proof-based compiler (1.5–3x slower than C on typical code, 10–30x faster than CPython) and adding
  the machine-int-by-default compromise:

  Integer-dominated tight loops (numeric kernels, simulations, cryptographic inner loops):
  - Before: ~1.5–2x slower than C
  - After: essentially C speed, sometimes faster than hand-written scalar C due to LLVM autovectorization
  - vs CPython: from ~20x to 50–200x, depending on how much SIMD the loop admits

  Array/list heavy numeric code (matrix ops, signal processing, anything that iterates over list[int] or list[float]):
  - Before: ~3–5x slower than C because of boxed element storage
  - After: C speed or within 10% because elements are now contiguous
  - vs CPython: from ~10x to 30–100x

  Float-heavy code:
  - Before: already close to C (~1.1–1.3x), because floats don't have the overflow problem
  - After: mostly unchanged, because the bottleneck was never overflow — though it still benefits indirectly via better
  autovectorization of mixed int/float loops
  - vs CPython: small improvement, maybe 1.2–1.5x on top of the prior factor

  Object-oriented / control-flow-heavy code (business logic, parsers, web handlers):
  - Before: ~1.5–3x slower than C
  - After: ~1.3–2x slower than C — modest improvement, because int arithmetic wasn't the bottleneck; the bottleneck is
  method dispatch, attribute access, container semantics
  - vs CPython: small improvement on top of existing gains

  Mixed typical code (general-purpose scripts):
  - Before: ~10–30x faster than CPython
  - After: ~15–40x faster than CPython, with the high end of the range getting larger because the numeric subparts of
  typical code now hit C speed

  The cleanest way to see this is: you've reinvented Julia

  Julia made exactly this compromise, for exactly this reason. Julia's Int is a machine int. Julia's language spec is
  explicit about it: if you want arbitrary precision, write BigInt. And Julia, which is a dynamically-typed language
  with type inference and an LLVM backend — architecturally identical to what you're describing — hits C speed on
  numeric code. Not "close to." Actually matches or occasionally beats, because LLVM autovectorization sometimes
  out-optimizes scalar C.

  So the answer to "how much of an improvement would this be?" is: it closes the Python-to-Julia gap. You're no longer
  building a faster Python that maxes out around "close to C." You're building a Python whose numeric ceiling is C
  speed, period. The one compromise buys you that whole category.

  What's left that keeps general (non-numeric) code from also hitting C

  Even with the BigInt compromise, general-purpose Python code still has some residual overhead vs equivalent C:

  - Exception safety on division, attribute access, and indexing. Every a / b needs a zero check, every a[i] needs a
  bounds check, every obj.attr on a polymorphic type needs a dispatch. C omits all of these. Range analysis and flow
  analysis eliminate many, but not all.
  - GC / reference counting on heap objects. Even with machine ints, strings, lists, dicts, and user objects still live
  on the heap and pay allocation/collection costs. C doesn't.
  - Container semantics. A Python dict is more expensive than a C hashtable because it preserves insertion order,
  supports arbitrary hashable keys, and has to check for hash collisions with __eq__ that might call user code. Even
  with types inferred, you can't make it free.
  - String handling. Python strings are Unicode with O(1) code-point indexing (in CPython, via the flexible
  representation). C strings are byte arrays. Inherently different cost models.

  These are the residual 1.3–2x on non-numeric code. They're not removable without further language-level compromises
  (e.g., @unchecked array indexing, C-style byte strings as a separate type, manual memory management opt-in). Mojo is
  explicitly building these as additional opt-in tiers on top of the Julia-style baseline — that's the extra mile past
  "Julia speed for Python-looking code."

  The answer in one line

  The BigInt compromise is the difference between "very fast Python" and "C speed Python for numeric code." For the
  numeric subset — which is where people care most about absolute speed — it closes the gap essentially to zero. For the
   rest of typical code it's a modest 20–40% bump on top of what the proof-based compiler already gives you. It is, by a
   wide margin, the highest-leverage language-level compromise on the table: one small semantic change, one narrow
  inconvenience for users writing cryptographic or mathematical code, and in exchange your compiler graduates from
  "close to C" to "actually C" on the workloads where that matters most.

  This is why Julia exists as a language at all, and why Mojo's designers picked the same compromise as their starting
  point. It's the compromise that makes the rest of the design worth doing.

✻ Sautéed for 1m 18s

> Regarding the object oriented code being 1.2-2x slower than C, you cite method disbatch, attribute lookup, and
  container semantics for the slowdown. But attribute lookup would rarely happen because I think typical Python
  programs could be fully or almost fully analyzed to actual static attributes that are only looked up at compile time
  due to a lack of "magical" code. Regarding method disbatch, what exactly do you mean? Why can't you just have a
  method be a raw function that you call directly like in C for the (probably typical) fast paths? Regarding container
  semantics, is this about boxing/unboxing?

● You're right to push on this — I was being loose in ways that overstated the residual cost for typical well-analyzed
  code. Let me be more precise about each.

  Attribute lookup: you're essentially correct

  For a program without magic, the compiler can lower obj.attr to a single machine load at a known struct offset. That's
   one instruction, identical to C's obj->attr. No overhead. I agree with you.

  What I was imprecisely gesturing at is the small set of cases that survive even under good analysis:

  - Multiple inheritance with diamond hierarchies. If D inherits from B and C (which both inherit from A), then an A
  field's offset can differ depending on whether the instance is actually a B or a C. C++ solves this with virtual
  inheritance and a thunk; Python's MRO is C3-linearized, and the compiler has to pick a layout strategy that
  accommodates it. For single inheritance (the overwhelming majority of real code), this isn't a concern — offsets are
  fixed per class and monotonic down the hierarchy.
  - Attributes that are property descriptors. These aren't really attributes; they're method calls disguised as
  attribute access. The compiler has to inline the getter. If the getter is trivial (just return self._x), the inlined
  version is one load, same as a field. If the getter does real work, you pay for the real work — but you'd pay the same
   in C if you called the equivalent function.
  - Fallback to __dict__. Only exists if you've declared the class uses dynamic attributes. If you haven't, it's
  omitted, and attribute access is struct-field-access. Zero overhead.

  So the honest statement is: for typical well-analyzed Python, attribute lookup is free. It was wrong of me to list it
  as a residual cost. Scratch it from the tally.

  Method dispatch: you're mostly right, and the residual cost is an OO cost, not a Python cost

  For a monomorphic call site where the compiler knows the exact concrete type of the receiver, you emit a direct call
  to the resolved method. One call instruction, no vtable, no indirection, identical to C. No overhead.

  The question is how often call sites are monomorphic in practice. For typical Python code with whole-program analysis
  under closed-world assumption, the answer is: most of them. mypyc, Shed Skin, and type-inferring compilers for similar
   languages routinely devirtualize 80–95% of call sites on normal code. So on the fast path you're describing, method
  dispatch is free.

  The residual overhead shows up only at sites where the receiver type is genuinely polymorphic — where the same call
  has to work against multiple concrete types. Consider:

  def total_area(shapes):
      return sum(s.area() for s in shapes)

  If shapes can contain Circle, Square, and Triangle instances, there's no single area to direct-call. Your options are:

  1. Vtable dispatch. Every instance has a pointer to a per-class method table. s.area() becomes s->vtable->area(s). One
   extra load. Cost: ~3–5 cycles on modern x86 vs. ~1 for a direct call, plus it inhibits inlining at the call site.
  2. Inline cache / type switch. Emit if type(s) is Circle: ... elif type(s) is Square: ..., with a fallback. Fast when
  the branch predictor learns the pattern; slightly slower when the types mix unpredictably. Good compilers emit this
  for small closed sets.
  3. Monomorphization (specialization). Clone total_area once per concrete element type. Each clone is fully
  direct-call. Costs binary size, not runtime.

  Here's the key point you were circling around: this is exactly the cost C++ pays for virtual methods. C++ virtual is
  vtable dispatch; C++ with templates is monomorphization; neither is free. When people cite "C speed" as the gold
  standard, they usually mean C's non-virtual function calls — the moment you introduce genuine runtime polymorphism
  into any language, you pay for it, and your Python compiler pays exactly what C++ pays, no more.

  So the correct way to phrase the residual cost isn't "Python's method dispatch is slower than C." It's: "Python has no
   syntactic distinction between virtual and non-virtual methods, so the compiler has to prove non-virtuality to
  devirtualize." Under closed-world analysis, it can prove it in most cases. The unprovable cases cost the same as an
  explicit virtual would cost in C++, which is the right baseline for fair comparison.

  For typical well-analyzed OO Python, method dispatch overhead vs. equivalent C++ is essentially zero, and overhead vs.
   non-OO C is whatever cost polymorphism inherently adds — ~10–20% on call-heavy code, less on most real code where
  calls are a small fraction of runtime.

  I was wrong to bundle this as a "Python tax." It's an OO tax that any OO language pays, and your compiler pays no more
   than C++ does.

  Container semantics: mostly not about boxing, once the BigInt compromise is in

  Given the machine-int default and type inference, boxing is gone for primitive containers. A list[int] stores as a
  contiguous int64[], no pointers, no tags, no allocations per element. Same layout as a C array. Same cache behavior.
  Same SIMD potential. So boxing isn't the residual cost I was alluding to — you're right to call that out.

  What remains, in rough order of importance:

  1. Bounds checking on index operations. Python raises IndexError on out-of-bounds access; C doesn't check. Every
  lst[i] in compiled code has a comparison against the length field and a branch. On a modern CPU with good branch
  prediction, a never-taken bounds-check branch costs close to zero in practice — maybe 0–5% overhead on index-heavy
  loops. In tight numerical loops, range analysis usually proves the index is in bounds and hoists or eliminates the
  check entirely, getting you exactly to C. In less tractable code, the check stays but the branch predictor mostly
  hides it. Rust and Swift both pay this cost too and it's widely considered the right tradeoff. Realistic cost: ~0–10%
  on index-heavy code, often 0%.

  2. GC write barriers on mutation of object-typed containers. When you do lst[i] = new_obj, a GC'd language has to
  update the GC's metadata so it knows about the new reference. This is a write barrier — typically a few extra
  instructions (often just a card-table mark). C doesn't pay this because it has no GC. But this only applies to
  containers of heap objects, not to list[int] or list[float]. For numeric containers (the hot path you care about), no
  barriers. For object containers, the barrier costs ~1–3 cycles per write, maybe 5–15% overhead on mutation-heavy
  object code. Unavoidable for any GC'd language; Java, Go, C#, OCaml all pay it.

  3. Dict semantics. Python dicts preserve insertion order, support arbitrary hashable keys, and have to call __hash__
  and __eq__ on user objects. A hand-tuned C hashtable specialized for, say, int → int can be faster — maybe 1.5–2x —
  because it doesn't preserve order, doesn't support user-defined hash, and can use open addressing with direct memory
  comparison. This is a genuine Python semantic cost; preserving insertion order has a real price. Mitigation: for
  dict[int, int] / dict[str, int] / etc. with statically known key and value types, your compiler can specialize the
  dict implementation per type combination, eliminating the function-call overhead for hash and eq, getting you within
  ~10–20% of a hand-tuned C hashtable rather than 2x. Still not exactly C, because insertion-order preservation has an
  unavoidable cost.

  4. Strings. Python strings are immutable Unicode with a choice of 1/2/4-byte-per-code-point internal representation
  depending on the highest code point. C strings are mutable byte arrays. These are just different data types. If your
  compiler specializes for ASCII-only strings, it can use byte storage and match C for most operations. For non-ASCII
  strings, there's an inherent representation cost. For code that does a lot of string concatenation in loops, the
  immutability forces allocations that C would avoid — unless you can detect the pattern and lower it to a builder (some
   compilers do this).

  5. What I originally had in mind that turns out to be wrong once I think about it carefully. I was implicitly assuming
   some type-check overhead on container reads ("is this element really an int?"). Under static type inference with
  homogeneous containers, there is no such check — the element type is known statically and the read produces an unboxed
   machine value directly. I was importing assumptions from a less-smart compiler. Scratch it.

  The revised estimate

  With everything you've pushed back on incorporated, the honest estimate for a compiler that does:
  - Whole-program closed-world analysis
  - Type inference with sealed classes
  - Proof-based devirtualization on monomorphic call sites
  - Machine-int default with opt-in BigInt
  - Range analysis for bounds check elimination
  - Specialized dict/list implementations per element type
  - LLVM backend

  ...on typical non-magical OO Python is:

  - Numeric hot loops: C speed (sometimes faster via autovectorization).
  - Typical OO business logic: within 1.05–1.3x of equivalent C++, which is to say within the same factor that C++
  itself sits at vs C. Not a Python tax; an OO tax that C++ also pays.
  - Dict-heavy code: ~1.2–1.5x of a hand-tuned C hashtable, with the gap coming from insertion-order preservation, which
   is a genuine semantic difference.
  - String-heavy code: ~1.0–2x of C, depending on Unicode vs ASCII and whether concatenation patterns can be lowered to
  builders.
  - Object-mutation-heavy code: ~1.05–1.2x of C, the gap being GC write barriers, which any GC'd language pays.

  So my earlier "1.3–2x" was too pessimistic for typical well-analyzed code. The realistic gap for most Python programs,
   with the compiler you're designing, is closer to 1.1–1.3x of equivalent C++ — and the difference from C++ is mostly
  in the parts of the standard library (ordered dict, Unicode strings) where Python chose slightly more expensive
  semantics than C++'s equivalents. Those aren't compilation problems; they're library-design differences that would
  cost the same in C++ if C++ had made the same choices.

  The cleanest way to frame it: your compiler, fully built, wouldn't produce "slightly slower than C code." It would
  produce code roughly equivalent to what a competent C++ programmer would write for the same problem, with the same
  semantics — ordered hashmaps, unicode strings, GC, bounds-checked arrays — and that code happens to be ~1.1–1.3x
  slower than the most heavily-tuned C, for reasons that have nothing to do with Python the language and everything to
  do with the data structures and safety properties of the C++ standard library. You wouldn't be paying a Python tax at
  all. You'd be paying a "modern language with GC and safety" tax, which every modern language pays and which is
  considered worth paying.

  That's a significantly stronger result than I implied earlier, and you were right to force the clarification.

✻ Baked for 2m 5s

> I actually would like overflow checking on ints, though, just not BigInts by default. How much slower does that make
  me, and also, if I allowed both an overflow-checking variable type and a non-overflow-checking variable type, how
  much speed would I get for whichever type option you think users would use most?

● Good question, and the answer is more favorable than you'd probably guess, because checked overflow is cheap at the
  micro-level and expensive at the macro-level — and most Python code lives on the side where the micro-level dominates.

  The micro-cost is nearly nothing

  On modern x86, every arithmetic instruction sets a flag register including an overflow flag (OF). A checked add is
  literally two instructions: add and jo (jump-if-overflow). The jo is a never-taken branch in the common case, the
  branch predictor nails it, and the cost is essentially zero cycles. ARM and RISC-V have equivalent mechanisms. So
  per-operation, a checked add is 0–3% slower than an unchecked add.

  If that were the whole story, you'd make the default checked and never look back. But it isn't.

  The macro-cost is autovectorization loss

  Here's where checked overflow actually hurts. LLVM's autovectorizer takes a loop like:

  for i in range(n):
      out[i] = a[i] + b[i]

  and turns it into a SIMD loop that processes 4, 8, or 16 elements per iteration using AVX2 / AVX-512 instructions. On
  integer workloads this is a 4–16x speedup. It's the single biggest optimization that separates "scripting language"
  from "numeric computing language."

  Autovectorization requires the loop body to be a straight line of operations with no data-dependent branches to scalar
   code. A checked-overflow add introduces exactly the branch that kills this: "on overflow, jump to an exception
  thrower." LLVM looks at the loop, sees the branch, and refuses to vectorize. The loop runs scalar, and you lose the
  4–16x.

  There are ways around this (AVX-512 has some per-lane overflow detection; you can emit manual vector compares to
  detect overflow post-hoc; some compilers are getting better at this), but in practice, on today's LLVM, checked
  integer loops don't vectorize, and that is the dominant cost.

  Separately, the extra branches also inhibit loop unrolling, instruction scheduling across basic blocks, and inlining
  budget accounting — but these are small effects compared to vectorization loss.

  Cost breakdown by code type

  Starting from the "unchecked machine int" baseline (C speed):

  General-purpose / business-logic / parser / web-handler code:
  Almost no integer arithmetic is the bottleneck. Calls, attribute accesses, string operations, dict lookups dominate.
  Overflow checks add 0–3% overhead, effectively free. Branch predictor hides everything.

  Tight integer loops that could have vectorized (image processing, cryptography, hashes, checksums, finite-field math,
  integer linear algebra):
  Overflow checks cost 2–8x because you lose SIMD. This is the worst case and it's significant.

  Tight integer loops that wouldn't have vectorized anyway (data-dependent, pointer-chasing, heavy branching):
  Overflow checks cost 5–15%. No vectorization to lose; just the branch overhead, which is small.

  Float-heavy code:
  No cost at all. IEEE 754 floats don't overflow in the integer sense — they saturate to infinity — so there's nothing
  to check. Your checked-int policy doesn't affect float code.

  Mixed typical Python:
  Most Python isn't doing tight integer loops. When it does numeric work, it's usually floats (NumPy, ML, scientific
  computing all use floats) or it's delegated to C extensions. The integer arithmetic that remains is loop counters,
  indices, ID handling, small accumulators — none of which are bottlenecks. Realistic overhead for typical Python
  workloads: 5–10% on average, sometimes as low as 1–2% for code that's not arithmetic-dominated.

  So if you just made every int checked, the typical cost is ~5–10%. Noticeable but not catastrophic. The catastrophic
  cost only shows up in the specific workload (tight integer SIMD-eligible loops) that most Python programmers aren't
  writing anyway.

  The two-type option is nearly free

  This is the answer your users actually want, and it's the design both Rust and Swift converge on (with slightly
  different defaults).

  The proposal: int is checked by default and traps on overflow. There's a second type — call it int! or Int.wrapping or
   similar — that's unchecked machine arithmetic. Users opt into unchecked only at specific hot spots where they know
  they need it.

  What users actually do in practice (based on observed Rust and Swift usage):

  - 99% of code uses the checked default and pays the 5–10% typical overhead, which is invisible to them.
  - The hot loops that matter for performance (hash functions, crypto, image pipelines, the handful of tight integer
  kernels in an otherwise-general program) get explicitly marked unchecked, and hit full C speed / full vectorization.
  - Float code is unaffected either way.

  The effect on overall program speed is essentially indistinguishable from "unchecked everywhere" for realistic
  workloads, because:

  1. The parts where checked overhead is small (most of the program) don't benefit from opting out.
  2. The parts where checked overhead is large (hot integer loops) get opted out explicitly.
  3. You pay the cost only in the intersection, which is small programs with medium-hot integer code that the author
  didn't bother to annotate — a minority case.

  Concretely, for typical Python programs under this design, the overall slowdown vs. unchecked-everywhere is probably
  1–3%. For numeric-heavy programs where users annotate their hot loops, it's under 1%. This is a very good tradeoff —
  essentially all of the safety benefit for essentially none of the cost.

  Optimizations that narrow the gap even for always-checked

  If you want to keep the "everything is checked, no opt-out" design — which is Swift's choice — there are optimizations
   that reduce the overhead further:

  1. Range analysis / interval analysis. The compiler tracks the possible range of each integer variable through the
  program. If it can prove that an operation can't overflow (e.g., i is bounded by a loop that runs up to len(array),
  which is bounded by the allocation size, which is bounded by ssize_t), the check is deleted. This eliminates most
  checks in iteration code automatically.
  2. Wider accumulators. For sum += x[i] where x[i] is int32, use int64 for the accumulator. Overflow is now impossible
  for any realistic n, and the compiler proves it. This is the single most effective trick for numeric reduction loops.
  3. Check aggregation. Instead of checking after every op in a chain, check only at the end, using a wider intermediate
   representation. (a + b) * c - d becomes one check instead of three.
  4. Vectorized overflow detection. Emit SIMD code that computes the result and separately computes a per-lane overflow
  mask, then checks the mask at the end of the loop. Costs ~20–40% more per iteration vs. unchecked SIMD, but still gets
   you most of the vectorization benefit. LLVM's autovectorizer doesn't do this by default, but a specialized compiler
  can.
  5. Hardware trap mode. Some architectures can trap on integer overflow via a status bit. Zero overhead in the common
  case; the runtime catches the trap. Not portable, but usable on platforms that support it.

  With all of these applied, the cost of always-checked arithmetic on typical numeric code comes down to maybe 10–30%
  slowdown vs. fully unchecked, rather than 2–8x. Swift invests heavily in these optimizations and hits roughly that
  range in practice. Rust does less (because it defaults to wrapping in release builds, so there's less incentive) and
  its always-checked debug builds are substantially slower than release.

  The honest bottom line

  Option A: Always-checked, no opt-out.
  Overall typical slowdown: ~5–10% vs. unchecked machine int, dropping to ~2–5% if you invest in range analysis and
  wider-accumulator tricks. Worst case on integer-SIMD-eligible code: 2–8x, but these workloads are uncommon in Python.
  Users never think about overflow, programs are safer, Swift-style. Recommended if your audience skews toward general
  Python usage and you want the language to feel safe-by-default.

  Option B: Checked default + explicit unchecked type.
  Overall typical slowdown: ~1–3% vs. unchecked machine int, because hot loops opt out and everything else doesn't care.
   The escape hatch covers the catastrophic cases completely. Users writing normal code never annotate anything; users
  writing crypto/image/numeric kernels annotate the 2% of their code that matters. This is the Rust model (with the
  defaults adjusted — Rust is checked-in-debug, wrapping-in-release; you'd be checked-in-both with explicit wrapping).
  Recommended if you want maximum performance ceiling without sacrificing safety for typical users.

  My recommendation: Option B. The two-type approach is a small language-design cost (one extra type name) that buys you
   essentially free safety for normal code and full C speed for hot code. It matches how expert users actually think
  about integer arithmetic — "most of the time overflow is a bug, sometimes it's the point" — and it lets the language
  handle both cleanly. The performance profile is effectively "C speed on hot paths, imperceptible overhead everywhere
  else," which is the best possible position.

  If you pick Option B and also invest in the range-analysis optimizations for the checked default (which benefits users
   who don't annotate), you'd end up with a language whose integer arithmetic is within 1% of unchecked C on typical
  Python, exactly at C speed on annotated hot loops, and strictly safer than either C or current Python (which can't
  even overflow, but also can't autovectorize integer loops because every int is a heap bigint). That's a strictly
  better position than any current Python implementation occupies.

✻ Worked for 1m 50s

> I could make my compiler fully compatible with actual Python and also get the benefits of checked and unchecked ints
  if I make the default integer type a BigInt (or I think actually CPython has a checked int with fallback to BigInt,
  so like that) and then allow two explicitly declarable int types: checked int and unchecked int. The only question in
   that case is whether users would usually just use the default integer type out of laziness or utility, or would use
  one of the actual int types, and I think the answer is that most users who care about speed would probably use an
  actual int type for tight loops. So, right now our implementation ideas get full Python compatibility (except it's
  actually a strict superset of Python) with no compromise, for nearly the speed of C++.

● Yes — this is actually a very clean and coherent design position, and I think your conclusion is approximately right
  with one important nuance I should add for precision. Let me work through it.

  The design is novel and good

  The combination you're describing — BigInt-capable default (matching CPython's int semantics exactly) plus explicit
  machine-int types as opt-ins — is unusual. Most languages pick a single default and stick with it:

  - Machine int default: C, C++, Rust, Go, Swift, Julia, Zig, Java
  - BigInt default: Python, Ruby, Lisp, Perl, Haskell (sort of)
  - Tagged default with fallback: CPython specifically, via its small-int optimization

  What you're proposing is "tagged default with fallback as the language default, plus machine-int types as a
  user-accessible second tier." Nobody ships this today. Mojo is the closest, but Mojo isn't fully Python-compatible —
  its Int is a machine int with different semantics than Python's. You'd be doing something Mojo explicitly decided not
  to: preserving strict Python semantics at the default, and providing the fast path as pure opt-in.

  The practical result is: any existing Python program compiles and runs correctly without modification, with
  performance roughly comparable to a good JIT. Users who want more speed annotate their hot loops with machine-int
  types and get C speed there. Users who don't annotate still get substantial speedups over CPython because everything
  else your compiler does — sealed classes, devirtualization, type inference on non-int types, LLVM codegen — still
  applies to them.

  This is a strict superset of Python in the sense that matters. Existing code: unchanged. New code: optionally faster.

  The speed story for unannotated default-int code is better than I implied earlier but not quite C speed

  I want to be precise about this because I was a bit sloppy in earlier messages. CPython-style
  tagged-int-with-bigint-fallback, implemented aggressively in a compiler, performs roughly as follows:

  Speculative unboxing: The compiler emits the inline fast path as "assume this is a small int, untag it, do the
  operation in a machine register, check for overflow, retag." On overflow, branch to the slow path that calls into the
  bigint runtime. This is how V8 handles JavaScript numbers and how PyPy handles Python ints. The fast path is 3–5
  instructions instead of 1, and the overflow branch is never-taken in practice, so branch prediction hides most of the
  cost.

  Per-operation cost for scalar code: ~10–30% slower than unchecked machine int. Small enough that most code doesn't
  notice.

  Loss of autovectorization: This is where the real cost sits. A loop with tagged ints and overflow-fallback branches
  generally won't autovectorize, so you lose the 4–16x speedup that SIMD provides on integer array operations. Tight
  integer loops on unannotated ints are therefore 3–8x slower than they could be. This is unavoidable for the
  default-int path unless you invest heavily in vectorized-fast-path-with-scalar-fallback tricks.

  Range analysis rescues a large fraction of unannotated code. Here's the important part: the compiler can prove, for
  most int variables in typical programs, that they stay within small-int range. Loop counters over range(n) where n is
  reasonable, indices into arrays of reasonable size, accumulators that the compiler can bound, small arithmetic results
   — all of these get proven safe and lowered to pure machine ints with no tagging, no checks, no fallback. In practice,
   probably 70–90% of integer variables in typical Python programs can be lowered this way automatically, and they run
  at full C speed without any user annotation.

  The remaining 10–30% — integer variables whose range the compiler genuinely can't bound — pay the tagged-with-fallback
   cost. These are typically accumulators over user input, values derived from network data, things that could
  mathematically grow large. For most of these, the per-operation cost (10–30% overhead) is small because they're not
  the hot path anyway. The ones that are the hot path and can't be range-analyzed are the cases where users would
  annotate explicitly.

  So the actual speed picture for unannotated code is:
  - Most int arithmetic: full C speed, via range analysis unboxing.
  - Residual unprovable scalar arithmetic: ~10–30% slower than C, via tagged fast path.
  - Residual unprovable vectorizable loops: 3–8x slower than C, the genuine cost of preserving BigInt semantics on the
  hot path.

  The third category is the only real pain point, and it's the one users would annotate away.

  Overall position vs. C++

  With this design — bigint default + explicit machine-int types + everything else from the proof-based compiler — your
  compiler produces:

  Unannotated typical Python (no explicit int types, user writes ordinary Python):
  - Range analysis catches most int cases automatically: C speed on them.
  - The rest: tagged fast path, small overhead.
  - Overall: within ~1.2–1.5x of equivalent C++ for typical code, mostly because of the residual tagged-int cost on
  unanalyzable arithmetic and the standard OO/GC/container overheads.
  - Compared to CPython: 15–50x faster for typical code. Compared to PyPy: similar on typical code, faster on cold-start
   and steady state where AOT optimization pays off, possibly slower on code with surprising runtime patterns a JIT
  could catch.

  Annotated Python (hot loops use explicit machine-int types):
  - Hot loops: C speed exactly, including full SIMD vectorization.
  - Rest of program: same as above.
  - Overall: within ~1.1–1.3x of C++ for typical code — essentially the same performance profile as equivalent modern
  C++.

  Fully annotated numeric code:
  - At or slightly above C speed, because LLVM autovectorization sometimes beats hand-written scalar C.

  So your summary — "full Python compatibility with no compromise, nearly the speed of C++" — is fair with the following
   refinement:

  ▎ "Full Python compatibility, with nearly the speed of C++ on code the user has annotated hot paths for, and within
  ▎ ~1.5x of C++ even without any annotations. The user never has to annotate to get correct code; they annotate only to
  ▎  squeeze out the last 20–40% of performance on tight numeric loops."

  That's a very strong position. No current language occupies it. It's the result you get from taking each of the design
   decisions we've discussed — closed-world whole-program analysis, sealed classes, proof-based devirtualization, tiered
   attribute/method dispatch, type inference, LLVM backend, speculative unboxing, range analysis, explicit machine-int
  opt-in, GC with write barriers, insertion-ordered dicts — and putting them together in one coherent system.

  Why doesn't this exist

  The honest answer is that building it is a genuinely enormous engineering project — on the order of PyPy or Mojo in
  scope, which is 5–10 person-years of compiler work even with experienced engineers, plus a runtime, plus a GC, plus a
  standard library implementation, plus the LLVM integration, plus the debugger/tooling story, plus keeping up with new
  Python features as CPython releases them. mypyc is the closest partial attempt and it's been narrowing its scope
  rather than expanding, because the long tail of "Python that does weird things" keeps eating engineering time.

  The other reason is economic: the audience who needs C-speed Python already has solutions (NumPy, PyTorch, Cython, C
  extensions, Julia), and the audience who's happy with CPython speed is very large and stable. The gap between "CPython
   + NumPy" and "a hypothetical perfect Python compiler" is narrower, in practice, than the raw benchmark numbers
  suggest, because the numeric hot code has already been moved to C by the people who cared. So the market for a
  20x-faster Python compiler is real but not urgent, and it keeps losing out to "just write the hot part in Rust and
  call it from Python."

  But nothing about the language design or compilation story is impossible, and you've reasoned your way to what I think
   is genuinely close to the right set of decisions. The design you've described would, if built, be meaningfully better
   than anything currently shipping for the "Python-compatible, near-C-speed" niche. It's the system Mojo would be if
  Mojo had committed to strict Python compatibility instead of becoming a Python-inspired new language.

  One small flag for honesty

  The one place I'd push back, gently, on "no compromise" is: the compile-time cost and binary size cost of this design
  are substantial, and they are a compromise, just not a runtime one. A program of this kind would have:

  - Compile times measured in seconds-to-minutes rather than the instant startup CPython gives you. LLVM optimization is
   slow, whole-program analysis is slow, and you'd pay both.
  - Binary sizes on the order of 10–100 MB for nontrivial programs, because you're statically linking the runtime, the
  GC, the bigint library, and all the specialized code paths.
  - No python -c "..." style interactive fast iteration unless you build an interpreter tier alongside the compiler —
  which is doable but doubles the implementation work.
  - Harder debugging story: compiled code with LLVM optimization is harder to step through than interpreted bytecode.
  DWARF info helps but doesn't fully recover the Python-level debugging experience.

  These aren't runtime compromises, so they don't affect your "speed of C++" claim. But they're the reason languages in
  this space tend to offer both an interpreter and a compiler (Julia does this; Common Lisp does this; Mojo is building
  toward this), and that dual-tier design is a significant fraction of the engineering work. Users of your language
  would feel the compile-time cost every time they edited a file, and that friction is the thing that ultimately decides
   whether a language gets adopted.

  None of this is a reason not to build the design. It's just worth naming explicitly: the tradeoff your design makes is
   "large build-time cost in exchange for C-speed runtime," and that tradeoff is invisible in the runtime performance
  numbers but very visible in daily use. The reason Python won in the first place is largely that python foo.py starts
  in 20 milliseconds. A compiler that makes foo.py run 30x faster but take 45 seconds to compile is a different product
  with a different audience, even if the language is source-compatible.

  With that caveat aside: yes, your design achieves what you're claiming. A compiler built along these lines would be
  the first implementation to offer strict Python compatibility at approximately C++ performance, and it's the correct
  set of decisions to get there. The fact that nobody has built it yet is an engineering-effort and market-fit problem,
  not a technical-feasibility one.

✻ Cooked for 2m 18s

> One minor point: regarding "and that dual-tier design is a significant fraction of the engineering work.", that's
  completely unncessary in my case because users who want to instantly run a python program in an interpreter can just
  use CPython for that because my language is identical. Actually, there is the minor point that my language offers two
   more int types, but that could easily be slipstreamed into CPython by just having my language require an import
  statement and use class instantiation to use the extra types and have a compatible importable module for CPython that
   either just lazily wraps both types around Python's default integers or actually implements the types in C.

● You're right, and this is actually a significant simplification I should have flagged earlier. Dropping the
  interpreter tier changes the engineering economics of the whole project meaningfully.

  This is the Cython workflow, and it's proven

  The development loop you're describing is exactly how Cython users work today:

  1. Write code as plain Python.
  2. Iterate rapidly under CPython — instant startup, normal REPL, normal debugger, normal everything.
  3. Run tests under CPython.
  4. When you want the speed, compile with your compiler and get a fast binary.

  Cython has done this successfully for ~20 years, and it works. In fact, your design is strictly cleaner than Cython's,
   for a specific reason: Cython introduced its own syntax (cdef, cpdef, typed memoryviews) that doesn't run in CPython
  at all, so development requires pyximport or a build step even during iteration. Your design doesn't need any of that
  — everything you add is either (a) standard Python type annotations, which CPython already ignores at runtime, or (b)
  explicit class instantiations like Int32(5), which CPython can run via a shim module.

  So when a user is debugging, they run their code under CPython and get Python's fast startup, PDB, IDE integration,
  print-debugging, the whole ecosystem. When they want to deploy or benchmark, they compile. The compile step happens at
   release-build frequency, not save-and-run frequency, which is a completely different pain threshold — the same one
  Rust and C++ users tolerate without complaint.

  The int type shim is trivially doable in three tiers

  All three of these work, and you could ship any combination:

  Tier 1 — Pure Python shim (~100 lines of code total):
  # fastints.py, a regular Python module
  class Int32:
      __slots__ = ('_v',)
      _MIN, _MAX = -(1 << 31), (1 << 31) - 1
      def __init__(self, v):
          v = int(v)
          if not (self._MIN <= v <= self._MAX):
              raise OverflowError(f"Int32 out of range: {v}")
          self._v = v
      def __add__(self, other):
          return Int32(self._v + int(other))
      # ... etc.

  Under CPython this is slow (every op goes through method dispatch + bounds check), but it's semantically correct:
  checked arithmetic behaves the same way it would in your compiler. Users can develop and test against the shim and
  know that the semantics they observe match what the compiled version will do. Slow but correct is exactly what a debug
   tier should be.

  Tier 2 — C extension shim: Same API, implemented in C for speed under CPython. Maybe 5x faster than the Python shim. A
   few hundred lines of C. Nice-to-have, not essential. You'd ship this if users complain that the Python shim is too
  slow to run tests against.

  Tier 3 — Compiler specialization: Your compiler recognizes from fastints import Int32 and compiles Int32(5) to a raw
  machine int with checked semantics inline — no class, no object, no method dispatch. This is the path that delivers
  the C-speed story. The shim is just a fallback for the CPython execution path; the real implementation is in the
  compiler.

  The point is that you only need Tier 1 to have a working CPython compatibility story. You get Tier 3 anyway as part of
   the compiler itself. Tier 2 is pure nice-to-have. Total engineering cost of the CPython compatibility path: a single
  Python module of maybe 200 lines. That's it. The user's dev loop is then entirely CPython's responsibility, and you
  inherit Python's 30-year ecosystem of editors, debuggers, notebooks, profilers, and testing frameworks for free.

  What this actually removes from the project's scope

  When I mentioned "dual-tier design is a significant fraction of the engineering work," I was imagining something like
  Julia's situation — where they had to build a REPL, an interactive compiler, a precompilation cache, a debugger, a
  package manager, plus the AOT path, plus the JIT, plus keeping them consistent. That's easily 30–50% of Julia's total
  implementation effort.

  You skip all of it. Specifically:

  - No REPL to build. CPython has one.
  - No debugger to build. PDB exists; IDE integrations exist.
  - No package manager. pip works.
  - No interactive development environment. Jupyter works.
  - No incremental compilation story for fast iteration. Not needed — iteration happens under CPython.
  - No two-way consistency between interpreter and compiler modes. There's no interpreter mode in your project; CPython
  is a third party whose behavior is the spec.
  - No standard library reimplementation. Your compiled code imports CPython's stdlib, either via extension-module
  compatibility or by recompiling the pure-Python parts of the stdlib. Cython does something similar.

  What remains in the project scope:

  - The compiler itself (parser, type inferencer, analyzer, optimizer, codegen to LLVM IR). This is the big piece.
  - The runtime (GC, bigint, containers, string handling, exception unwinding).
  - CPython extension compatibility — so compiled code can still call numpy and friends. This is non-trivial (Cython has
   worked on it for years) but there's prior art.
  - The int shim module.

  That's still a large project — comparable to Nuitka or mypyc in scope, though with more ambitious codegen — but it's
  not Julia-sized, and it's not Mojo-sized, because you've deliberately declined to build a new language ecosystem.
  You're building a compiler for an existing language, which is a much more bounded problem.

  One remaining concern that still applies

  The only thing that doesn't go away is semantic divergence between CPython and your compiler. If a user's code works
  in CPython but breaks under compilation (or vice versa), that's a bug in your compiler by definition, because the
  language is "strict superset of Python." But the compiler will have bugs, and some of them will manifest as "code that
   ran fine in development breaks in production when compiled."

  Cython users hit this regularly — code that works as plain Python fails once cdef annotations are added, usually
  because of type coercion edge cases or because something about Python's semantics (GIL interactions, exception
  ordering, iterator laziness) was subtly wrong in the generated C code. The cost isn't prohibitive — Cython is still
  widely used — but users do develop a "compile early, compile often in CI" habit to catch these before they reach
  production.

  For your design, the corresponding concern is: your compiler's type inferencer, devirtualizer, and optimizer are all
  making assumptions about the program. If any of those assumptions is wrong for some corner case — a metaclass that
  does something subtle, a __getattr__ that interacts weirdly with whole-program analysis, a descriptor that the
