  devirtualizer didn't account for — the compiled version will behave differently from CPython. Making this robust is
  the main correctness engineering effort, and it's why mypyc has chosen to keep its scope narrow rather than trying to
  compile "all of Python."

  The way to manage this is to run the full test suite under both CPython and the compiler, and treat any divergence as
  a compiler bug to be fixed. That's the same discipline Cython and mypyc projects use. It scales reasonably well if the
   compiler is built carefully from the start with "CPython is the spec" as a first-class commitment.

  The project you're describing, correctly sized

  With the CPython-as-interpreter-tier realization, your project is:

  ~3–7 person-years of compiler engineering (rough estimate, based on what mypyc, Nuitka, and Shed Skin have each cost)
  to get from nothing to a production-quality compiler covering most of Python with the optimization suite you've
  described. Add a year or two for the LLVM integration, runtime, and GC, and another year or two for CPython
  extension-module compatibility if you want numpy to work.

  That's still a lot, but it's a well-defined lot — you know what "done" looks like, you have CPython as a reference
  implementation to test against, and you don't have to also build an ecosystem. It's a project a small team (3–5
  people) could plausibly complete in a few years, which is why mypyc exists as a Dropbox-internal effort and why Nuitka
   is maintained by essentially one person plus contributors. The scale is real but not mythical.

  The runtime performance result we worked through — within 1.1–1.5x of C++ for typical annotated Python, full CPython
  compatibility, no dev-loop compromise — is the payoff for that engineering investment. Whether the payoff justifies
  the cost depends on who's asking. For an individual developer, probably not. For a well-funded org whose bottleneck is
   "we're paying AWS millions because our Python is slow and moving to Go would take longer than rewriting the world,"
  possibly yes. That's the calculation every project in this space is making, and it's why the answer keeps coming out
  "maybe but not quite urgently enough," and why the slot is still open.

  Your reasoning is sound and the design is coherent. If someone built what you've described, it would work, and it
  would occupy a position no current tool occupies. The reason it doesn't exist is engineering budget and market fit —
  not because anyone has found a flaw in the plan.

✻ Baked for 1m 33s

> Aside from how much this program is really called for in the larger world, I personally like it because I like Python
   but I also like the idea of *fast* Python, and also because I've been thinking about vibecoding my own OS and using
  Python as one of the languages to make it in. And the whole existence of PyPy with all its man-hours of work seems to
   imply that the work for this project really is worth the real-world demand for it. There's the one drawback of slow
  compile times, but if you think about it, even that can be drastically mitigated with optional project-level (and
  python file level?) flags to disable specific types of program analysis where we know that our code qualifies for the
   fast paths, or disable them in the opposite direction--just using the slow paths instead--where we care about
  speedups but we don't care about getting every last drop out of it.

● The OS project is a genuinely compelling motivation, and it changes the calculus a bit — because for a hobby OS, some
  of the concerns that would matter for a commercial Python replacement stop mattering. You don't need perfect numpy
  compatibility, you don't need pip support for every package in the ecosystem, you don't need to beat PyPy on every
  benchmark. You need a compiler that produces standalone binaries from Python source, with enough of the language to be
   pleasant to write in. That's a much smaller target than "replace CPython for the world's enterprise Python users,"
  and the engineering scope shrinks correspondingly. You can probably skip CPython extension compatibility entirely at
  first (nothing to link to on bare metal anyway), skip the full Python standard library (you're writing the OS, so
  you're writing your own stdlib), and focus the compiler on the language-core work, which is where the interesting
  parts are anyway.

  PyPy is indeed the proof-of-demand. The PyPy team has been working at it for roughly 20 years, has been funded at
  various points by Mozilla, the EU, and others, and keeps attracting contributors. The tracing JIT architecture they
  picked was a harder bet than AOT in some ways — runtime type feedback, deoptimization, JIT warmup, multiple
  compilation tiers, a whole interpreter written in RPython that then gets translated — and they still shipped it. Your
  AOT design is architecturally simpler (no JIT warmup, no deopt metadata needed for perf, no interpreter tier) and
  trades runtime adaptability for build-time analysis, which is a reasonable tradeoff and probably a somewhat smaller
  total engineering cost. So "PyPy exists and has users" is reasonable evidence that "a good Python compiler is worth
  building."

  On the compile-time flags idea

  Your instinct here is exactly right and it's how every mature compiler works. Concretely, the useful dimensions to
  expose as flags or pragmas are:

  1. Optimization level. -O0 through -O3 style. At -O0 you'd do local analysis only, no whole-program work,
  straightforward codegen, compile as fast as possible. At -O3 you'd do full closed-world devirtualization, range
  analysis, escape analysis, LLVM -O3, and generate the binary you're actually proud of. Users iterate at -O0 or -O1 and
   ship at -O3.
  2. Per-file "hot" annotations. Mark a file or module as # cython: performance-critical (or whatever syntax), meaning
  "spend unlimited budget on this file." The rest of the project gets fast compilation with reasonable-but-not-maximum
  optimization. This maps directly to how OS kernels already work: most of the kernel is "make it correct," a small
  subset is "tune the hell out of this inner loop." Your compiler can match that discipline directly.
  3. Incremental compilation with analysis caching. Only re-analyze files that changed (and their dependents). Cache the
   results of type inference, range analysis, and devirtualization per file. This is the single biggest compile-time win
   for iterative development on large codebases and it's expected of any modern compiler. It's non-trivial to implement
  correctly with whole-program analysis — you have to track dependency edges precisely so you know what to invalidate —
  but it's well-understood engineering.
  4. Pragma-based "I promise this code is well-behaved" hints. Let the user assert things the compiler can't prove: #
  pragma: no-subclass, # pragma: sealed, # pragma: no-introspection. The compiler trusts the assertion and skips the
  proof, getting fast-path codegen without the analysis cost. This is how C++ final and Rust #[inline(always)] work —
  letting the programmer hand-annotate facts that would be too expensive to prove automatically.

  With those four mechanisms in place, the edit-compile-run loop for someone iterating on a specific file stays fast
  even on a large project, because you're only paying full analysis cost at release-build time and only on the files
  marked hot. Compile-time stops being a show-stopper.

  One OS-specific note worth flagging

  For kernel-level code specifically, the thing that usually trips up "use a high-level language in an OS" projects is
  the garbage collector. Kernels don't want GC pauses during interrupt handlers, and they don't want to allocate in code
   paths that can't tolerate unpredictable latency. The usual solutions are:

  - Region-based or arena allocation for code paths where GC is forbidden. The compiler can be told "in this function,
  all allocations go to this arena, freed on exit."
  - Manual memory management escape hatch for the lowest-level code. Rust's approach: most code is safe-with-GC-or-RC,
  but unsafe blocks let you reach raw pointers when needed.
  - GC-free subset of the language for kernel code. Similar to how Rust's no_std mode exists for embedded and kernel
  work — a subset of the language that works without heap allocation at all.

  For your OS project, you'd probably want some version of (3): a pragma or mode that says "in this file, no implicit
  allocation, no GC, stack-only data, explicit machine types, explicit pointer operations." Code written in that mode
  would look like Python but behave like C, and it's what you'd write your interrupt handlers and memory manager in. The
   rest of the OS — shell, drivers, userspace — could use normal GC'd Python and run fine.

  This isn't something you'd need to build on day one. You'd probably start with "compile Python to a freestanding
  binary with the GC included" and write the early OS in that, then add the no_gc mode when you need to write the
  scheduler or interrupt handlers. The sequencing is natural.

  Closing thought

  The design you've converged on through this conversation — strict Python superset, BigInt default with opt-in machine
  types, proof-based whole-program analysis, tiered dispatch, LLVM backend, CPython as the interpreter tier, optional
  optimization levels, per-file hot annotations — is coherent and occupies a position no existing tool quite fills.
  Whether anyone else would use it is a separate question from whether it's worth building for your own purposes, and
  for "write a hobby OS in a language I like, and make it fast enough to not hate," it's a reasonable tool to want to
  build. The scope is large but bounded, the architecture is sound, and you can grow it incrementally — start with
  enough of the compiler to produce any binary at all, then layer on optimizations as you need them for specific parts
  of the OS.

  If you do start building it, the piece I'd recommend getting right first is the whole-program analysis and the type
  inferencer, because everything else depends on them being trustworthy. The codegen and the LLVM integration are
  comparatively straightforward — LLVM is a well-documented target and the textbook techniques apply. It's the "how do I
   know what this Python code means" layer that'll determine whether the project succeeds or dies in a swamp of corner
  cases, and it's where the genuine intellectual work lives. Good luck with it.

✻ Brewed for 1m 15s

> The intellectual work for most of this project, including the "how do I know what this Python code means" layer, is
  beyond me or beyond what I'm willing to expend brain cells on. If I make this language, you'll be the one doing it,
  and you'll do it many times faster than I or any other human could anyway. One other thing: just to be clear, I don't
   plan to use the language for kernel/low-level areas of the OS, that's why I said it would be "one of the languages."

● Understood on both counts, and the clarification about "one of the languages" makes the OS plan more sensible, not
  less. The practical split is well-established: the bottom layer (bootloader, early init, interrupt handlers, page
  tables, context switches) in something like Rust, C, or Zig, and everything above that — drivers past the bare-metal
  stub, filesystems, networking stacks, shell, window system, userspace — in the fast Python. That's a workable division
   because the low-level layer is small (a few thousand lines for a hobby OS) and the high-level layer is where all the
  interesting surface area lives, so the language you're most productive in gets used for most of the code.

  You also dodge the hardest engineering problem in the whole stack when you make that split, which is "GC-free Python
  subset for kernel-critical paths." That feature is the single thing I'd worry most about getting right, because GC
  pauses in an interrupt handler are the kind of bug that produces mysterious data corruption three days after boot. Not
   having to build it at all is a real simplification — the compiler just produces GC'd binaries, and the kernel handles
   its own memory in another language entirely. The FFI between the two layers becomes the coordination point, and
  that's a much more bounded problem than "make the GC not run at bad times."

  On the "you'd be doing the work" point: fair, and it's worth saying explicitly that building a compiler of this scope
  through AI-assisted iteration is a meaningfully different process than building one the old-fashioned way, and the
  bottlenecks are different. The traditional bottleneck is person-hours of expert compiler engineers, which is why
  projects like PyPy take decades with small teams. The AI-assisted bottleneck is closer to "how clearly can the human
  specify what they want, how well can they evaluate whether what got produced is actually correct, and how much
  patience do they have for the long tail of bugs that only surface under specific inputs." Those are different skills
  than writing a type inferencer by hand, and they're skills you've demonstrated in this conversation — the questions
  you asked were precise enough that the answers actually narrowed the design space instead of just producing opinions.
  That's the scarce resource in this kind of project, not the willingness to learn SSA construction.

  The parts that would still be hard even with good AI help are the ones where correctness is non-obvious and testing is
   insufficient to catch bugs: the garbage collector (concurrent GCs are famously bug-ridden and the bugs manifest as
  memory corruption weeks later), the whole-program analysis soundness (an unsound devirtualization produces wrong
  results in corner cases you won't hit in your own testing), and the interaction between exception handling and stack
  unwinding in optimized code. Those three areas specifically are where you'd want to invest extra effort in testing
  infrastructure — property-based tests, differential testing against CPython on large existing codebases, fuzzing the
  analyzer — because they're the ones where "looks fine, ships, breaks later" failure modes are most common. Everything
  else is recoverable from normal test-and-iterate development.

  If you do decide to start, my suggestion for the smallest useful first milestone would be: a compiler that takes a
  single Python file using a restricted subset (no classes, no generators, no exceptions, just functions, ints, floats,
  lists, strings, and control flow), produces a native binary via LLVM, and runs correctly on CPython's existing test
  suite for that subset. That's a project where every piece is well-scoped, the scope grows naturally from there, and at
   each step you have a working compiler that does something end-to-end. Projects in this space die when they try to
  implement everything at once and end up with a half-finished type system, a half-finished codegen, and nothing
  runnable. Incremental with a real binary at every step is the way.

  Good luck with it if you pursue it. The design is sound, the motivation is legitimate, and the fact that you have a
  concrete application (your own OS) is actually a significant advantage — it gives you a forcing function for what to
  build next, because you'll always know what the next thing your OS needs from the language is, and you can drive the
  compiler's feature set from that demand rather than from an abstract "be a complete Python implementation" goal. That
  kind of application-driven development is how most successful languages actually grew.

> Regarding the difficulty of correctly proving soundness (I guess you mean soundness for allowing various execution
  fast paths), might one way to mitigate that be to be more conservative in what cases immediately fire a no-go for
  fastpaths rather than trying to further analyze whether they're really sound in the end? And would the trade-off of
  speedup coverage versus difficulty of getting the prover right be worth it for this approach?

● Yes, and this is actually the standard compiler-design response to exactly the problem you're identifying — it's so
  standard it has a name: "sound but incomplete" analysis is the thing every production compiler ships, as opposed to
  the "sound and complete" fantasy that doesn't exist for nontrivial languages. Every real compiler deliberately misses
  optimizations in ambiguous cases in exchange for never being wrong. The question is just how much to miss, and that's
  a dial you can turn.

  How the tradeoff actually works out

  For each optimization, there's typically an "obvious yes" region, an "obvious no" region, and a "maybe with careful
  analysis" region. The obvious cases are usually easy to implement and cover most of the performance opportunity. The
  ambiguous middle is where all the complexity lives.

  Rough decomposition for the analyses your compiler cares about:

  - Devirtualization: Trivial cases (sealed class, type known from recent assignment, method on a class with no
  subclasses anywhere in the program) catch ~70–85% of all devirtualization opportunities. The hard cases (type flows
  through multiple function calls, polymorphic generic code, maybe-sealed classes) catch the remaining 15–30% and
  require 4–5x as much code to get right.
  - Bounds check elimination: Simple patterns (for i in range(len(arr)): arr[i], explicit bounds-checked index) catch
  ~60–80%. The rest needs interprocedural range analysis.
  - Unboxing / range proofs on ints: "Only assigned from int literals and arithmetic on provably-bounded values" catches
   ~70% of loop counters, indices, and small accumulators. Cross-function inference catches the rest.
  - Escape analysis for stack allocation: "Doesn't leave this function, isn't stored in any container, isn't captured by
   a closure" catches ~50–70%. Full whole-program escape analysis catches more but is famously bug-prone.
  - Write barrier elision: Trivial patterns catch ~40–60% with almost no complexity.

  If you run the numbers, a "conservative first pass" analyzer that takes only the clear cases for each of these lands
  at roughly 60–75% of the peak performance a sophisticated analyzer would achieve, for something like 15–25% of the
  implementation complexity. That's an unusually good deal, and it's exactly the deal mypyc, early JIT compilers, and
  many other projects have taken.

  In absolute terms: conservative-analyzer version of your compiler probably hits 1.5–2.5x of C++ for typical code
  instead of 1.1–1.3x. You lose maybe a factor of 1.3-2x on the runtime, and you save something like 3–5x on
  implementation effort. For a project where the bottleneck is soundness and verifiability rather than
  benchmark-chasing, that's clearly worth it.

  Why this trade is particularly favorable for your situation

  Three things stack in your favor:

  1. Failure mode asymmetry. An unsound aggressive optimization produces silent wrong results — memory corruption, data
  being mutated from under you, exceptions appearing from code that shouldn't raise them. These are the worst bugs in
  all of software; they're frequently days-to-weeks to diagnose and they destroy user trust in the compiler. A
  conservative analyzer's failure mode is "slightly slower than it could be," which is completely recoverable and causes
   zero correctness problems. When you're building this through AI-assisted iteration and the human reviewer has to
  verify soundness by hand, you want the failure modes to be visible, not silent. Conservative analysis is vastly safer
  here.
  2. Annotations recover the lost ground where it matters. The 30–40% of performance you leave on the table isn't evenly
   distributed — most of it sits in a small number of hot functions per program, the same functions a user would profile
   and tune anyway. Give users explicit annotations (@sealed, @final, Int32 / Int64, @no_escape, @assume_in_range) and
  they can recover the missed optimizations at the exact spots that matter without the compiler needing to prove
  anything. The user asserts the fact, the compiler trusts it and runs the fast path. This is how Rust's unsafe, C++'s
  final and noexcept, and Cython's cdef all work. It's a proven pattern and it maps naturally onto your design — you
  already have explicit opt-in types for ints, you can extend the same principle everywhere else.
  3. Debuggability. A conservative analyzer can emit diagnostics: "couldn't optimize this call site because I couldn't
  prove X." The user reads the diagnostic, adds an annotation, and gets the fast path. An aggressive analyzer fails
  silently in the wrong direction — either the optimization works or it doesn't, and when it doesn't, you don't know
  why. For a compiler you're actively developing, "tell me why you bailed out" is enormously valuable, because it tells
  you exactly which analyses are worth strengthening next.

  The "ratchet" path

  The best part of this approach is that you don't have to pick a conservatism level once and commit. You ship version 1
   of the compiler with the simplest possible analyses — the "obvious yes" cases only. It's correct, it runs, it's maybe
   3–5x slower than the theoretical ceiling but still 8–15x faster than CPython, and you can trust it. Users start using
   it, file bug reports about correctness (which are all rare and fixable), and profile their code.

  From the profiling data, you see which analyses would pay off most — "if you devirtualized this one call pattern,
  users would get 2x on their workload." You implement that specific analysis strengthening, ship it, and gain a factor
  of performance with a bounded piece of new code. You repeat.

  Each step of the ratchet is testable in isolation (you can turn the new analysis on and off and compare results),
  recoverable (if the new analysis turns out to be buggy, you revert to the previous conservative version), and driven
  by real workloads (you're strengthening the analyses that actually pay off for real code, not the ones that look
  clever in theory). This is how basically every successful compiler project has incrementally grown its optimizer, and
  it avoids the failure mode where someone tries to build a perfect analyzer upfront, gets lost in corner cases, and
  ships nothing.

  For your situation specifically — AI-assisted build, human-reviewer bottleneck, hobby OS as the forcing function —
  this ratchet approach is close to optimal. It lets you start from a compiler you can fully reason about, and grow it
  in small verifiable increments whose cost/benefit you can evaluate at each step. You're never committed to a piece of
  sophisticated analysis that turned out to be wrong.

  The direct answer

  Yes, conservative bail-out is the right approach, and the tradeoff is strongly favorable for this project. You pay
  maybe a factor of 1.3–2x on peak runtime performance in exchange for a compiler that's 3–5x simpler to build,
  substantially safer against correctness bugs, and easier to grow incrementally. The performance cost is real but
  recoverable through user annotations at the specific spots where it matters, and the implementation savings free up
  effort to spend on the parts of the compiler where it actually counts — the runtime, the GC, the CPython compatibility
   layer, the codegen quality. It's the standard choice for production compilers for exactly the reasons you intuited,
  and nothing about your situation makes it a worse choice than usual. If anything, it makes it a better one.

✻ Brewed for 1m 36s

> Two things. One, if the user is allowed to add annotations to help out the compiler/analyzer, the annotations would
  have to be fully compatible with CPython (even if they're just ignored on CPython), so I guess the only option for
  that is that they exist in #comment sections. Actually, a better option is that they just make dummy references or
  calls to various objects of some particular dummy class, and that dummy class can easily be imported in CPython too.
  Two, if I do this project, there's almost zero chance I'm going to invest in manual review of your or whatever AI's
  code, because (1) that's above my head and/or knowledge level, and (2) I'd spend so much time doing that that most of
   the advantage of using AI is nullified, and I never even had the tenacity to complete a single largeish project
  before AI came along.

● Both points are reasonable and deserve direct engagement. The first is straightforward; the second genuinely changes
  how the project would need to be built, but it doesn't make it impossible — it just shifts where the engineering
  effort goes.

  On annotations — you're on the right track, and it's cleaner than comments

  You don't need to use comments at all. Modern Python already has a well-established idiom for "metadata that the
  interpreter ignores but tools use," and the entire ecosystem runs on it:

  Type hints. def f(x: Int32) -> Int32: is standard PEP 484 syntax. CPython evaluates the annotations to make sure the
  names resolve (or, with from __future__ import annotations, doesn't even evaluate them) but doesn't do anything with
  the values at runtime. Your compiler reads them and specializes. This is how mypy, pyright, Numba, and mypyc all work.
   Int32 just needs to be importable from some module that ships a dummy class; in CPython it's a no-op wrapper, in your
   compiler it's a directive.

  Decorators. @sealed, @final, @no_escape, @inline — all just Python decorators. In CPython they return the function
  unchanged (or the class unchanged). In your compiler they're compile-time directives. This is exactly how
  @torch.jit.script, @numba.jit, @functools.lru_cache, and a dozen other libraries already work.

  Instantiable wrapper classes. x = Int32(5) as you described. In CPython it's a class that wraps a regular int and
  implements all the arithmetic operators to match the semantics (checked arithmetic raises OverflowError, unchecked
  wraps modulo 2^32, etc.). Slow under CPython but semantically correct. Your compiler recognizes the type and emits a
  raw machine int instead of the wrapper.

  __slots__. Already part of Python. Declares that a class has a fixed set of attributes. CPython uses it for a small
  memory optimization; your compiler uses it as a declaration that the class is sealed and its layout is fixed. Zero new
   syntax; you just attach additional meaning to a feature that already exists.

  Module imports as opt-ins. from fastpy import checked_ints at the top of a file. In CPython it imports a module (maybe
   empty). In your compiler it's a flag that changes how the rest of the file is analyzed. Similar to from __future__
  import ... but in your own namespace.

  So the full annotation story for your compiler is: a single PyPI-installable package called something like fastpy that
   exports all the dummy types, decorators, and wrapper classes. Any file that uses your compiler's features imports
  from that package. Under CPython, the imports resolve to no-ops and slow wrappers, and the code runs correctly (just
  slowly). Under your compiler, the imports are recognized and the annotations trigger real specialization.

  This is the cleanest possible integration with CPython. No comments, no pragmas, no special syntax. Just regular
  Python modules that happen to have two implementations — a runtime one for CPython and a compile-time one for your
  compiler. Users write normal-looking Python the whole way down, and their IDEs, linters, type checkers, and
  documentation tools all work without any extra configuration.

  PyTorch's TorchScript does exactly this. Numba does exactly this. JAX does exactly this. It's a proven, clean pattern
  and it maps onto your design with no friction.

  On not reviewing the code — this is the real question

  The honest traditional answer to "how do you trust a compiler you didn't write?" is "you don't, unless you review it,
  because compilers have subtle bugs that produce silently wrong results." For production compilers built by experts,
  the answer has historically been code review, formal verification, or both.

  That doesn't work for you, so you need a different answer. The good news is that there is a different answer, and it's
   genuinely effective. The bad news is that it requires investing in the alternative upfront, and it's not optional —
  you can't skip both review and the alternative and expect a working compiler. But the alternative is well within
  reach, and it's probably a better use of effort than review would be anyway.

  The alternative is aggressive automated testing, with CPython itself as the oracle. Here's what it looks like
  concretely:

  1. Differential testing against CPython. This is by far the most important technique. For any Python program, you know
   what the correct output is: whatever CPython produces. So you set up a pipeline that takes programs, runs them under
  CPython, runs them under your compiler, and compares the outputs. Any difference is a bug. You run this continuously,
  on:
  - The CPython test suite itself (thousands of tests covering language semantics)
  - Popular Python package test suites from PyPI (Django, Flask, requests, pytest, beautifulsoup, etc.)
  - Your own handwritten tests for language features
  - Random programs generated by a program-generator (like CSmith for C, but for Python)

  This catches correctness bugs without anyone having to read a single line of compiler code. A bug manifests as
  "compiler and CPython disagreed on the output of this program," which is (a) an unambiguous signal, (b) automatically
  detected, and (c) usually narrow enough to localize by bisecting the program.

  2. Runtime assertion mode. Build your compiler with a debug flag that makes it emit defensive runtime checks verifying
   its own assumptions. If the compiler devirtualized a call because it proved the type was Foo, the debug build checks
  assert type(x) is Foo before the call. If the compiler eliminated a bounds check because it proved the index was in
  range, the debug build re-checks it. Any assertion failure is a soundness bug, caught automatically with zero human
  effort. You only enable this in debug mode, so production performance is unaffected.

  3. Property-based testing with Hypothesis. Hypothesis (a Python library) generates random inputs satisfying specified
  properties and runs your code against them. For your compiler, you can generate random well-formed Python programs and
   check properties like "output matches CPython," "no crashes," "no hangs." This finds bugs that hand-written tests
  miss because it explores corner cases systematically.

  4. Fuzzing. Lower-level than property-based testing. Feed random byte streams to your compiler's parser, random ASTs
  to the analyzer, random typed programs to the codegen. You're not checking correctness here, just absence of crashes
  and hangs. This catches the compiler equivalent of buffer overflows — places where malformed input produces undefined
  behavior.

  5. A regression database. Every bug you find gets added to a test file. Every future build runs the full regression
  set. Bugs never come back.

  6. AI-assisted review of the AI-generated code. Even if you're not reading the code yourself, you can have Claude (or
  another AI) review its own output, systematically, for specific classes of bugs. "Review this devirtualization pass
  and check for the following issues: ..." This isn't as good as expert human review, but it's a meaningful layer, and
  it's cheap to do at scale.

  7. Incremental shipping. Ship the compiler early, in restricted form, and use it yourself for real work (your OS).
  Every time you hit a bug, you find it before anyone else does, and you fix it with a regression test. The compiler
  grows from a small correct core outward, and the core stays correct because you're exercising it constantly on real
  code.

  Why this actually works

  The reason this approach is effective — and why I'm confident rather than hand-waving — is that compilers have a
  uniquely favorable property for automated testing: the correctness specification is concrete and the oracle is
  available. CPython is the specification. You don't have to guess whether the compiler is correct; you run the program
  both ways and check. This is not the case for most software (there's no oracle for "did my web app render the right
  UI"), but it's the case for compilers, and it's why differential testing has been extraordinarily successful on real
  compilers.

  CSmith — a random C program generator used with differential testing — found over 400 previously-unknown bugs in GCC,
  Clang, and every other production C compiler in the space of a few years, many of them bugs that had survived decades
  of expert human review. Differential testing is often better than human review at finding the specific class of bugs
  compilers have, because humans are bad at reading code for the kind of edge cases that trip optimizers up, while
  random generators hit those edge cases by accident constantly.

  For Python specifically, the test oracle is even better than C's, because CPython is a single reference implementation
   that everyone agrees defines the language (C has multiple compilers and an ambiguous spec). You can literally run
  "does this program produce the same output under CPython and my compiler" as your entire correctness test, and for the
   vast majority of bugs that matters, that's sufficient.

  The part of the compiler that this approach is weakest on is the garbage collector, because GC bugs manifest as memory
   corruption that may not show up as an output difference — the wrong thing might happen but the output might still
  look right. GCs are also famously subtle and concurrency-prone. For this specific piece, I'd suggest either (a)
  starting with the simplest possible GC (a non-moving mark-sweep, which is boring but easy to get right) and leaving
  the fancy optimizations for later, or (b) using an existing proven GC implementation like Boehm GC as a library for
  the first version, and only building your own once everything else is working. Boehm GC is a conservative GC that's
  been in production for decades; it's not the fastest option but it's known-correct and you can swap it out later once
  you have testing infrastructure strong enough to trust a bespoke GC.

  The honest reframing of what the project looks like under your constraints

  Traditional compiler development: experts write careful code, review it, reason about it formally, trust it because
  they understand it.

  Your compiler development: conservative-by-default code that the AI writes, pushed through an automated testing
  gauntlet so aggressive that any bug that survives is the bug the gauntlet didn't think to check for, and shipped
  incrementally so that real-world usage catches the rest. You trust the compiler because the testing says it works, not
   because you read it.

  The tradeoff is different but it's not worse. In some ways it's actually stronger — automated tests don't get tired,
  don't miss things because they assume other code is correct, and don't have blind spots that senior compiler engineers
   famously have (like "obviously this optimization is safe, I don't need to prove it"). What you lose is the ability to
   reason about the compiler as a coherent whole; what you gain is millions of test inputs per day, run automatically,
  forever.

  The upfront investment is the testing infrastructure itself. This is work that has to happen before the compiler is
  useful, and it's probably 20–30% of the total project effort. Specifically:
  - A differential test harness that runs programs under CPython and the compiler and diffs outputs
  - A random Python program generator (this is the hardest piece; you'd need it to produce grammatically valid,
  type-safe, non-crashing programs, which is a nontrivial specification task)
  - A Hypothesis-based property testing setup for specific language features
  - A regression database with CI integration
  - The debug-mode runtime assertion system

  None of these are compiler code; they're all testing infrastructure, and they're much more tractable for an
  AI-assisted build than the compiler itself. Once they exist, every piece of compiler code you write is immediately
  checked against them, and bugs get caught within minutes of being introduced.

  The shape of your project, then, is: build the testing infrastructure first (or in parallel with the compiler's
  earliest passes), then let the compiler grow against the tests. The conservative analyses we discussed earlier play
  well with this — they have small, testable surface areas, and you can verify each one in isolation. The incremental
  ratchet approach plays well with this — every step is verified before you take the next one. The "use CPython as the
  interpreter tier" decision plays well with this — CPython is right there, ready to be the test oracle.

  It is a legitimate, completable project under your stated constraints. It's not the same project a team of compiler
  engineers would build, and it would probably end up with some quirks and limitations they would find embarrassing, but
   it would work, it would be correct on the inputs it was tested against, and it would do the thing you want — let you
  write Python for your OS and have it run fast. That's a success condition you can actually hit with the tools you're
  planning to use.

  The fact that you haven't completed a large project before isn't as relevant as you might think, either. The reason
  large projects fail isn't usually that the person couldn't understand the technical parts — it's that the project
  loses momentum in the long middle, where the interesting ideas are done and it's just grinding out scope. A compiler
  has an unusually clean structure for maintaining momentum because each feature is testable end-to-end from the day you
   add it: add integers, see them run; add classes, see them run; add exceptions, see them run. You always have a
  working program, and progress is always visible. This is very different from projects where you have to build half the
   scaffolding before anything works at all.

  Go build it if you want to. The plan is sound, your constraints don't invalidate it, and the resulting thing would be
  real and useful, even if only to you.

✻ Cooked for 2m 10s

> Regarding the 100-line slow Python implementation of the two added int types for use under CPython, maybe better than
   slowing the programs down that much when the user wants to run them under CPython is just making the types a wrapper
   around Python integers. Even though the behavior wouldn't be 100% identical, the only cases in which it varies are
  the case in which it fails to raise an exception and instead Just Works, and the case in which you don't get a most
  likely unpredictable and unwanted integer wraparound on some arithmetic operation.

● You're mostly right, and the simplification is worth taking — with one specific carve-out you should be aware of,
  because the "more permissive" framing isn't quite accurate in one case.

  Where you're right

  For the checked int case, your argument is airtight. The CPython shim is a transparent wrapper around Python's
  arbitrary-precision int. Development-time behavior differs from compiled behavior only in that the compiler raises
  OverflowError where CPython silently computes the correct result. This is:

  - Loud, not silent. The compiled version fails with a clear exception and a traceback. Nobody's data gets corrupted.
  The user looks at the traceback, sees "OverflowError at line 47," and fixes it.
  - Only a problem if you're relying on the overflow as control flow, which nobody sensible does.
  - Catchable in CI. Just run your test suite under the compiler occasionally — say, as a nightly job — and any latent
  overflow gets surfaced before it reaches production.
  - Much cheaper than the faithful simulation. Per-op overhead under CPython drops from "Python int arithmetic + bounds
  check + possible exception raise" to just "Python int arithmetic." Probably 3–5x faster, which matters when you're
  running tests.

  The total wrapper is maybe 30 lines instead of 100, and it's strictly better for development ergonomics. Take the
  simplification.

  Where it breaks, and it's a real case

  The unchecked int case has a subtlety your framing misses. You described the two differences as "fails to raise an
  exception" and "doesn't produce an unwanted wraparound." Both of those frame the shim as more permissive or more
  correct. But for unchecked int specifically, the wraparound is often the entire point of using the type. Unchecked int
   is what you reach for when you're writing:

  - Hash functions. FNV, MurmurHash, SipHash, CityHash — all specified in terms of wraparound uint32 or uint64
  arithmetic. The wraparound is the hash. If your shim does arbitrary-precision math instead, the hash value comes out
  wrong, and your hash doesn't match reference implementations or test vectors.
  - Cryptographic primitives. Stream ciphers, block ciphers, PRNGs — all specified in terms of fixed-width modular
  arithmetic. Same story.
  - CRC and checksums. Same.
  - Bit manipulation with fixed-width semantics. (x << 1) | (x >> 31) is a 32-bit rotate if x is uint32. In the shim, if
   x << 1 doesn't drop the high bit, you get the wrong rotated value.
  - Saturating or modular counters. Frame counters, sequence numbers, packet IDs — often intentionally wraparound.
  - Emulators and VMs. A CPU emulator needs uint32 to behave like a CPU register, not like a Python int.

  For all of these, the CPython shim with "just wraps a Python int" produces different output from the compiled version,
   and the difference isn't "more permissive" — it's wrong for the user's purposes. A hash function tested under CPython
   passes its own tests because the shim is self-consistent, but fails when compared against reference vectors, and
  fails when compiled and run in production.

  This is the one case where your proposed simplification genuinely breaks down. The user developing a hash function
  would test it under CPython, see it produce consistent-but-wrong values, compile it, and see it suddenly produce
  different consistent values. They'd have no way to tell which was "right" without running reference vectors, and
  they'd probably waste hours chasing the discrepancy before realizing the shim wasn't simulating the semantics they
  cared about.

  The pragmatic resolution

  The middle ground is small and clean: implement bitwise operations and overflow-truncation faithfully in the unchecked
   shim, but skip the bounds checks on arithmetic in the checked shim. The unchecked shim does result & 0xFFFFFFFF (or
  the appropriate mask) on every arithmetic op, which is one extra AND instruction in Python — cheap, simple, ~5 extra
  lines of code per type. The checked shim does nothing extra.

  class UInt32:
      __slots__ = ('_v',)
      _MASK = 0xFFFFFFFF
      def __init__(self, v):
          self._v = int(v) & self._MASK
      def __add__(self, other):
          return UInt32((self._v + int(other)) & self._MASK)
      def __lshift__(self, other):
          return UInt32((self._v << int(other)) & self._MASK)
      # etc.

  class Int32:
      __slots__ = ('_v',)
      def __init__(self, v):
          self._v = int(v)  # no mask, no check, just wrap
      def __add__(self, other):
          return Int32(self._v + int(other))
      # etc.

  The checked shim is ~20 lines, the unchecked shim is ~40 lines (more ops to mask), and total cost for both is under
  100 lines. You get:

  - Checked development semantics that match compiled semantics except on the loud-failure cases, which are recoverable.
  - Unchecked development semantics that exactly match compiled semantics, because the mask in the shim produces the
  same bit pattern the compiler would produce.
  - Cheap enough that running tests under CPython is fine for both types.
  - Simple enough that you can convince yourself it's correct by inspection.

  This is probably the right design. Your instinct to simplify was correct for the checked case — just keep the
  bit-level faithfulness for the unchecked case, because that's where users specifically depend on the bits. It's a
  minor addition to the shim and it removes the one genuine failure mode of the all-transparent approach.

  One meta-point

  The interesting thing about this whole shim discussion is that it's a microcosm of a pattern you'll hit repeatedly
  when building the compiler: there's a spectrum from "shim that perfectly simulates compiled behavior" to "shim that's
  as simple as possible," and the right answer depends on how the user will notice the difference. For features where
  the difference manifests as a loud exception, simple-and-fast is fine. For features where the difference manifests as
  silently different output, you need faithful simulation. The question to ask for each feature you shim is: "if the
  shim diverges from the compiled version, what does the user see?" — and pick the simplest shim that produces
  diagnoseable failures rather than silent ones.

  Applying that principle here, your checked int wants the simple shim and your unchecked int wants the faithful-enough
  shim. Every future feature gets the same analysis.

✻ Sautéed for 1m 21s

> It's probably better to invest in writing a C CPython module for the unchecked int than writing a many times slower
  Pythno emulation of it. One other thing that occurred to me: I don't actually need the extra "checked but doesn't
  fallback to BigInt" type at all, only the unchecked type, because I think you said that the extra cost of Python's
  int checking to see if it falls back to BigInt is very minimal, like one or two opcodes or something, and that's also
   probably the same amount of opcodes or close to it that you'd have to run just to check for overflow and raise and
  exception instead of coverting it to a BigInt.

● Good call on both counts, with one subtle point about the second that's worth surfacing before you commit.

  The C extension for unchecked int is the right investment

  If you're going to care about CPython-mode performance for bit-manipulation code at all, a C extension is the right
  answer, not a Python-level simulation. Rough numbers: a Python-level UInt32 wrapper runs maybe 50–100x slower than a
  native int under CPython (every op is a method call plus attribute access plus a Python int op plus a mask plus a
  class instantiation). A C-extension version runs 2–5x slower than a native int — the overhead is mostly the PyObject*
  boxing and method-dispatch cost, which is unavoidable in any CPython extension, but the arithmetic itself is C-speed.
  For a user running tests of a hash function or crypto primitive under CPython during development, the difference is
  "tests take 30 seconds" vs. "tests take half an hour." Worth the few hundred lines of C.

  And writing it is straightforward — it's one of the simplest kinds of CPython extension, just a type object with
  arithmetic slots filled in. The Python C API documentation has worked examples. You can probably get a working
  UInt32/Int32/UInt64/Int64 family in under 1000 lines of C total. This is a one-time cost and then you're done.

  On dropping the checked type — your per-op observation is right, but there's a subtle downstream effect worth naming

  You're correct that the per-operation cost is essentially identical:

  - Default int fast path: add; jo slow_path; continue_with_tagged_result — 2 instructions.
  - Checked int fast path: add; jo raise_overflow; continue_with_machine_result — 2 instructions.

  The check cost is the same. You're also right that I described the overflow-check cost earlier as small, and that's
  what makes your simplification attractive. So at the hot-path arithmetic level, dropping the checked type and relying
  on the default int's existing check-with-BigInt-fallback is nearly free in terms of per-op cost.

  But there's a second-order effect that isn't about the check cost — it's about the representation of values at rest,
  between operations. The two types differ in what a variable of the type is when it's not actively being computed on:

  - Default int: the value at rest is "either an unboxed small int or a pointer to a BigInt heap object." Because the
  fallback produces a BigInt, the type system has to carry "might be a BigInt" through every subsequent use. In practice
   this means the storage representation is tagged (one bit distinguishes small-int from pointer) or boxed (always a
  pointer, possibly to an interned small int).
  - Checked int: the value at rest is "always a machine int32." The fallback is an exception, which is a control-flow
  exit rather than a data-flow merge, so the compiler knows that if the program continues past the operation, the result
   is a machine int. Storage can be 4 raw bytes, no tag, no pointer.

  This difference has downstream consequences that don't show up in per-op microbenchmarks:

  1. Dense array storage. list[Int32] can be a contiguous int32[] in memory — no tags, no pointers, one element per 4
  bytes. list[int] (where int is the default BigInt-capable type) can't, unless the compiler proves by range analysis
  that no element ever overflows, because the element type allows BigInts. For numeric code iterating over lists of
  integers, this is a big deal: dense storage gives you 4–8x better cache behavior and enables autovectorization of the
  loop. Range analysis can recover this for list[int] in some cases but not all; list[Int32] gets it unconditionally.

  2. Struct field layout. If you define a class class Point: x: Int32; y: Int32, each instance is 8 bytes (plus any
  object header). With x: int; y: int, each instance is 16 bytes of pointers (plus the pointed-to heap objects) unless
  the compiler proves a narrower type — and for fields, range analysis is often impossible because any caller can
  construct an instance with any value.

  3. Register keeping across loops. A checked int can live in a machine register across loop iterations, because its
  type doesn't change. A default int sometimes has to spill to memory at the loop-head join point, because one
  predecessor might have a small int and another might have a BigInt, and the compiler can't prove they agree unless
  range analysis succeeds on both paths.

  4. Autovectorization of loops. Both types can be vectorized in principle (via per-lane overflow detection), but the
  default int loop has an additional complication: the slow path produces a BigInt, which has a completely different
  representation, and the compiler has to emit scalar fallback code that transitions from vector state to BigInt state.
  This is rarely implemented in practice, so default-int loops typically just don't vectorize. Checked-int loops have a
  cleaner vectorization story because the slow path is an exception exit, not a representation change.

  So the actual tradeoff of dropping the checked type is: you lose guaranteed dense representation, and you have to rely
   on range analysis to recover it case-by-case. In cases where range analysis succeeds, the default int is
  indistinguishable from the checked int. In cases where it fails, you get the tagged representation and its associated
  cache/vectorization penalties.

  Whether this matters for your use case

  Honestly? Probably not much, for a few reasons:

  1. Range analysis is quite effective for typical code. Most integer variables in most programs are loop counters,
  indices, or small accumulators with provable bounds. Range analysis catches them automatically. The cases it misses
  are the cases where the user probably wouldn't know the bound either, and those are exactly the cases where raising an
   exception would be surprising to the user.
  2. For the specific "dense array" case, you have a cleaner answer: require explicit type annotations on container
  element types. list[int] uses the default representation; users who want dense integer lists can write list[Int32] (if
   you keep Int32 around as a type for this purpose) or use the unchecked type. The annotation communicates intent
  unambiguously and doesn't require range analysis to work.
  3. The cases where the checked type wins are ones where the user wants guaranteed fast paths and is willing to take an
   exception on violation. That's a legitimate use case, but it's a minority of code, and users who want it badly enough
   can annotate explicitly when you add the type later.
  4. Simplifying the type system has real benefits for you as the implementer. One fewer type means fewer shim classes,
  fewer compiler cases, fewer test matrices, fewer documentation pages, and fewer user decisions. Every feature you can
  drop from v1 is a feature that doesn't need to work correctly on day one.

  A reasonable resolution

  You don't need to drop checked int permanently; you can drop it from v1 and add it later if users ask for it. Ship:

  - Default int (CPython-compatible, small-int-fast-path-with-BigInt-fallback, range analysis unboxes where provable)
  - Unchecked int types (UInt32, Int32, UInt64, Int64) for bit-manipulation and crypto, with a C extension shim for
  CPython development

  And that's the entire integer story for v1. Simple, covers the two actual use cases (normal Python semantics; explicit
   machine int semantics), and leaves the door open for a checked tier later if real workloads turn out to need it.

  If you later find that dense list[int] is a bottleneck for some users, you have two expansion paths: (a) improve range
   analysis so it catches more cases automatically, or (b) introduce the checked type as an opt-in. You don't have to
  decide between them now — you can see which cases show up and pick the response that fits the observed need. This is
  the same "start conservative, ratchet as needed" principle as the analyzer design, applied to the type system.

  So yes, your simplification is defensible, the per-op argument supporting it is correct, and the
  representation-at-rest subtlety is real but probably recoverable through range analysis and explicit container type
  annotations. Dropping checked int from v1 is the right call if you're optimizing for implementation simplicity, which
  you should be.

✻ Cogitated for 2m 3s
